Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
Creation and Annihilation: Equivalence Questioned
Ghazali questions whether the impossibility of annihilation implies impossibility of creation ex nihilo, using the soul as example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the impossibility of annihilation does not automatically imply the impossibility of creation ex nihilo, and why the issue requires proof rather than self-evidence.

Core Idea
Annihilation and creation are not obviously equivalent; their relationship needs proof, not assumption.

In this passage, Ghazali challenges the philosophers' belief that both creation from nothing and annihilation into nothing are impossible. He examines two possible bases for this claim: analogy and experience. Through careful questioning, he shows that neither provides certainty, and that some Muslim philosophers already distinguish between the two, as in the case of the human soul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the philosophers argued that creation ex nihilo is impossible because annihilation is impossible. Ghazali questions the assumption that the two are similar.
This passage continues the discussion from the previous section.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting Ghazali's two-pronged questioning.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions.
3
Analyze the first possibility: analogy
Ghazali first considers whether the impossibility of creation is based on analogy with annihilation. He asks whether the two are truly equal.
He then points to Muslim philosophers who deny the annihilation of the soul as evidence of a distinction.
4
Analyze the second possibility: experience
Ghazali then considers whether the impossibility is based on direct experience. He questions whether such empirical impossibility is relative or absolute.
This raises doubts about certainty based on experiment.
5
Conclude the lesson
Understand that Ghazali has undermined both analogical and empirical arguments, leaving the issue open to further proof.
This sets up the need for metaphysical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا كنا قد تأكدنا - بوساطة التجربة - أن ما كان يظن إفناء وإعداماً، ليس إلا تحليلا وتفريقاً ، فماذا تأكدنا أن الإيجاد ليس إنشاء من عدم ، ولكنه تحويل من حال إلى حال ؟ هل قسنا الإيجاد من عدم ، على إعدام الموجود ، فقلنا : لما كان الثاني مستحيلا ، كما أفادت التجربة ، كان الأول مستحيلا ؛ لأنه مثله ، فالعجز عن الشيء ، عجز عن نظيره ؟ أم أخضعنا الإيجاد من عدم للتجربة ، كما أخضعنا إعدام الموجود للتجربة ، فظهر العجز عن الأول كما ظهر العجز عن الثاني ؟
وأن العجز عن
فإن يكن الأول ، فكأن الأمر مرده إلى القياس ، فهل المقيس عليه والمقيس ، متساويان تمام المساواة ؟ هل الإعدام مثل الإيجاد ، حتى يكون العجز عن الإعدام ، دليلا على العجز عن الإيجاد ؟ قد يكون حقاً أن تصيير الشيء إلى لا شيء ، مثل إيجاد الشيء من لاشيء، أحدهما ، دليل على العجز عن الآخر ، ولكن هل إدراك المساواة بينهما ، على فرض أنهما متساويان ، بديهى ؟ لو كان بديهيا ، لما قال بعض فلاسفة المسلمين ، إن النفس الإنسانية لا تفنى ، رغم أنها حادثة فصاحب هذا القول ، قد فرق بين الإيجاد والإعدام ، ولم يسو بينهما . وهذا يجعلنى لا أطمئن كل الاطمئنان ، إلى القول باستحالة إيجاد الشيء من لا شيء ، قياساً على عدم صيرورة الشيء إلى لا شيء . وهذا يعنى أن الأمر ليس بديهيا ، وإنما هو بحاجة إلى أدلة مقنعة
و إن يكن الثانى ، وهو أن الأمر مرده إلى التجربة ، وأنا قد حاولنا إيجاد شيء من لا شي . شيء ، فلم نستطع . فهل قضية عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من لا شيء وعدم إمكان صيرورة الشيء إلى لا شيء ، قضية نسبية ؟ أم قضية مطلقة ؟ أعنى أنه هل قضية عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من لاشيء ، وعدم إمكان
Then, if we have confirmed—by means of experience—that what was thought to be annihilation and destruction is only analysis and separation, then have we confirmed that creation (ījād) is not origination from nothing, but a transformation from state to state? Did we compare creation from nothing to annihilation of the existent, and say: since the second is impossible, as experience indicates, the first is impossible because it is like it—inability for a thing implies inability for its similar? Or did we subject creation from nothing to experience, just as we subjected annihilation of the existent to experience, and the inability for the first appeared just as the inability for the second appeared? And the inability for... If it is the first, then the matter reduces to analogy. So are the thing measured and the standard perfectly equal? Is annihilation like creation, such that inability for annihilation is evidence for inability for creation? It may be true that rendering a thing into nothing is similar to creating a thing from nothing, one of them being evidence for inability for the other. But is the perception of the equality between them—on the assumption that they are equal—self-evident? If it were self-evident, then some Muslim philosophers would not have said that the human soul does not perish, even though it is originated (ḥāditha). The holder of this statement has distinguished between creation and annihilation and did not equate them. This makes me not fully comfortable with the claim that creation of something from nothing is impossible by analogy to the non-becoming of something into nothing. This means that the matter is not self-evident; rather, it needs convincing proofs. If it is the second—that the matter reduces to experience—and we have tried to create something from nothing, and we could not; so is the proposition of the impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of rendering something into nothing a relative proposition or an absolute proposition? I mean, is the proposition of the impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of...
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage continues the argument against the self-evidence of the two impossibilities. Ghazali considers two possibilities. First, if we base the impossibility of creation ex nihilo on analogy with the impossibility of annihilation (since both seem similar), then we must ask whether the two are truly equal. The perception of equality cannot be self-evident; otherwise, Muslim philosophers who affirm that the human soul—though originated—does not perish would be contradicting themselves. But they are not, because they distinguish between creation and annihilation. Thus, the analogy fails, and the issue needs proof, not self-evidence. Second, if we base it on direct experience (having tried and failed to create something from nothing), then the impossibility becomes relative to our experience, not absolute. Ghazali ends with a question about whether the proposition is relative or absolute, implying that empirical grounds cannot yield absolute certainty. The passage thus undermines both the analogical and empirical arguments for the two impossibilities, showing that they are not self-evident and require deeper investigation.

Key Terms
إيجاد
إعدام
قياس
نفس إنسانية
حادثة
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If annihilation is impossible, then creation must also be impossible.
Correction: Ghazali shows that annihilation and creation are not equivalent; the impossibility of one does not automatically imply the impossibility of the other.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali bring up Muslim philosophers who say the human soul does not perish?
A) To prove the soul is eternal
B) To show that creation and annihilation are not equivalent
C) To argue that the soul is not created
D) To question the existence of the soul

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the impossibility of annihilation does not automatically imply the impossibility of creation ex nihilo; the issue requires independent proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold about God's power that actually come from assuming He is limited like you? Where in your life have you confused what you cannot do with what God cannot do? Commit today to identifying one such assumption and replacing it with what revelation teaches.
?
Ghazali points out that even learned philosophers can be inconsistent—some say the soul is created but never destroyed. Where in your own thinking have you been inconsistent, accepting one truth but rejecting its logical implications for your faith? Will you examine those inconsistencies honestly?
?
If empirical experience is not the ultimate judge of metaphysical reality, what does that mean for the things you have rejected simply because they seem beyond your experience? Is there a truth from revelation you have dismissed because it 'didn't make sense' to you? Decide now to submit that area of your mind to divine authority rather than your own limited observation.

The Impossibility of Creation from Nothing: Analogy or Experiment?
Ghazali questions whether the impossibility of creation ex nihilo is established by analogy or experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the basis for claiming the impossibility of creation ex nihilo, and why he argues it is not self-evident.

Core Idea
The impossibility of creation from nothing is not self-evident but requires proof, as analogy with annihilation falls short.

Ghazali challenges the assumption that humans cannot create from nothing is a universal truth. He examines whether this impossibility is known by direct experience or by analogy with annihilation. This passage reveals his philosophical method of questioning apparent certainties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get the overall flow of Ghazali's questioning without yet analyzing details.
Notice his use of rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Ghazali presents a dilemma: is the impossibility known by analogy or by direct experience? Locate these in the text.
3
Examine the critique of analogy
Ghazali questions whether annihilation and creation are truly equal such that inability in one implies inability in the other.
Look for the phrase 'هل الإعدام مثل الإيجاد'.
4
Consider the counterexample
Some Muslim philosophers affirm that the human soul does not perish, showing that they distinguish between creation and annihilation.
This undermines the intuitive equality.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that the impossibility is not intuitive (بديهي) but needs convincing proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا كنا قد تأكدنا - بوساطة التجربة - أن ما كان يظن إفناء وإعداماً، ليس إلا تحليلا وتفريقاً ، فماذا تأكدنا أن الإيجاد ليس إنشاء من عدم ، ولكنه تحويل من حال إلى حال ؟ هل قسنا الإيجاد من عدم ، على إعدام الموجود ، فقلنا : لما كان الثاني مستحيلا ، كما أفادت التجربة ، كان الأول مستحيلا ؛ لأنه مثله ، فالعجز عن الشيء ، عجز عن نظيره ؟ أم أخضعنا الإيجاد من عدم للتجربة ، كما أخضعنا إعدام الموجود للتجربة ، فظهر العجز عن الأول كما ظهر العجز عن الثاني ؟ وأن العجز عن فإن يكن الأول ، فكأن الأمر مرده إلى القياس ، فهل المقيس عليه والمقيس ، متساويان تمام المساواة ؟ هل الإعدام مثل الإيجاد ، حتى يكون العجز عن الإعدام ، دليلا على العجز عن الإيجاد ؟ قد يكون حقاً أن تصيير الشيء إلى لا شيء ، مثل إيجاد الشيء من لاشيء، أحدهما ، دليل على العجز عن الآخر ، ولكن هل إدراك المساواة بينهما ، على فرض أنهما متساويان ، بديهى ؟ لو كان بديهيا ، لما قال بعض فلاسفة المسلمين ، إن النفس الإنسانية لا تفنى ، رغم أنها حادثة فصاحب هذا القول ، قد فرق بين الإيجاد والإعدام ، ولم يسو بينهما . وهذا يجعلنى لا أطمئن كل الاطمئنان ، إلى القول باستحالة إيجاد الشيء من لا شيء ، قياساً على عدم صيرورة الشيء إلى لا شيء . وهذا يعنى أن الأمر ليس بديهيا ، وإنما هو بحاجة إلى أدلة مقنعة
Then, if we have confirmed - by means of experience - that what was thought to be annihilation and destruction is nothing but decomposition and separation, then have we confirmed that bringing into existence is not origination from nothing, but rather a transformation from one state to another? Did we analogize bringing into existence from nothing upon annihilation of the existent, saying: since the second is impossible, as experience indicated, the first is impossible because it is its like, so inability regarding a thing is inability regarding its counterpart? Or did we subject bringing into existence from nothing to experience, just as we subjected annihilation of the existent to experience, so inability regarding the first appeared just as inability regarding the second appeared? And inability regarding... If it is the first, then the matter reduces to analogy. Are the analogized and the analogized upon completely equal? Is annihilation like bringing into existence, such that inability regarding annihilation is evidence for inability regarding bringing into existence? It might truly be that making a thing become nothing is like bringing a thing from nothing; one of them is evidence of inability regarding the other. But is the perception of equality between them, assuming they are equal, intuitive? If it were intuitive, then some Muslim philosophers would not have said that the human soul does not perish, despite being originated. The proponent of this statement has distinguished between bringing into existence and annihilation and did not equate them. This makes me not entirely confident in the statement that bringing a thing from nothing is impossible, by analogy to the non-becoming of a thing into nothing. And this means that the matter is not intuitive, but rather it needs convincing proofs.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the basis for claiming the impossibility of creation from nothing (إيجاد من عدم). Ghazali begins by noting that experience has shown that what was thought to be annihilation is actually decomposition. He then poses a dilemma: Did we establish the impossibility of creation from nothing by analogy with annihilation (which experience shows is impossible), or did we subject creation from nothing to direct experience and find it impossible? If the first, then the argument depends on analogy. Ghazali questions whether annihilation and creation are truly equal such that inability in one implies inability in the other. He points out that some Muslim philosophers (like those who affirm the soul's survival) distinguish between the two, showing that the equality is not intuitive. Therefore, the impossibility of creation from nothing is not self-evident (بديهي) but requires proof.

Key Terms
إيجاد من عدم
إعدام
تجربة
قياس
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because humans cannot create from nothing, it is absolutely impossible in itself.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the impossibility is not self-evident; it may be based on analogy with annihilation, which is not necessarily equal.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali conclude about the impossibility of creation from nothing?
A) It is self-evident and needs no proof.
B) It is established by direct experience alone.
C) It is not intuitive and requires proof.
D) It is impossible even for God.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of creation from nothing is not self-evident; it requires reasoned proof, as Ghazali demonstrates by questioning the analogy with annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali admits he is 'not entirely confident' about a belief many hold as obvious. Is there any belief about God or creation that you hold simply because it is commonly taught, without ever questioning its basis? Will you examine it as he did?
?
The philosophers who believed the soul does not perish distinguished between annihilation and creation, showing that people can disagree on fundamental analogies. When you make an assumption about God's power, do you ever test your own analogies? What false equivalence might you be guilty of?
?
If the impossibility of creation from nothing is not self-evident and needs convincing proofs, how committed are you to seeking rational understanding of your faith, even when it challenges comfortable positions? Will you take the time to study such proofs, or rely on inherited certainty?

Absolute vs. Relative Impossibility of Annihilation
Ghazali distinguishes absolute impossibility from impossibility relative to human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's crucial distinction between what is impossible in itself and what is impossible only relative to human power, and why this invalidates the philosophers' argument that God cannot annihilate the universe.

Core Idea
Not every impossibility for creatures is an impossibility for the Creator.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali sharpens a key distinction. The philosophers argue that making the universe from nothing is impossible, using human incapacity as a proof. Ghazali asks: impossible in itself or only for humans? The answer determines the validity of their argument. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Ghazali uses rhetorical questions to structure his argument.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Ghazali presents two possible meanings of 'making a thing into nothing': either it is impossible in itself, or only impossible relative to human power. Find these two options in the text.
3
Understand the critique
Notice how Ghazali critiques both options: if relative, the argument changes the subject; if absolute, the proof is not general. Then he introduces the exception of essential impossibilities.
Essential impossibilities like combining opposites apply to both God and creatures.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction matter for the debate on creation? How does it weaken the philosophers' case?
This connects to the larger theme of divine omnipotence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصيير الشيء إلى لا شيء تعنى أن ذلك غير ممكن في ذاته ؟ أم أنه غير ممكن فقط بالنسبة إلى قدرة البشر ؟ فإن يكن الثانى ؛ وهو أن المراد بيان عدم إمكان ذلك بالنسبة لقدرة البشر · فهو تحول عن أصل موضوع النزاع وإن يكن الثانى ؛ وهو أن المراد أن ذلك غير ممكن في ذاته ، فكيف يستدل على حكم عام بدليل غير عام ؟ وهل يسوغ الانتقال إلى الحكم بعدم قدرة الخالق على ذلك ، من عجز قدرة المخلوق عليه ؟ نعم إن القدرتين قد يتساويان بالنسبة لبعض الأمور ، كالمستحيلات الذاتية ، مثل الجمع بين النقيضين ، أو رفعهما ، فهذه مما لا تتعلق بها قدرة الخالق ، ولا تدركها قدرة المخلوق ؛ ولكن ليس كلما عجزت قدرة المخلوق ، لم تتعلق قدرة الخالق ؛ لهذا يسوغ الإصغاء إلى من يتخذ من عجز البشر عن إيجاد شيء من لا شيء دليلا على استحالة الأمر في ذاته
Making a thing into nothing: does that mean it is impossible in itself? Or is it only impossible with respect to human power? If it is the second — that what is meant is to show its impossibility with respect to human power — then it is a shift from the original subject of dispute. And if it is the second — that what is meant is that it is impossible in itself — then how can one infer a general judgment from a non-general proof? And is it permissible to transfer from the incapacity of the creature's power to the judgment that the Creator has no power over that? Yes, the two powers may be equal with respect to some things, like essential impossibilities, such as combining two opposites or negating them; these are things with which neither the power of the Creator nor the power of the creature is engaged. But not every power that a creature lacks is such that the power of the Creator is not engaged with it; therefore it is permissible to listen to one who takes the incapacity of humans to create something from nothing as evidence for the impossibility of the matter in itself.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage begins with a distinction between two possible meanings of 'making a thing into nothing': either it is absolutely impossible in itself, or it is impossible only in relation to human power. Ghazali then examines both options. If the latter is meant, then the argument changes the subject of dispute. If the former is meant, then he questions how one can derive a general ruling (about the Creator's power) from a proof that is not general (based on human incapacity). He then explains that while Creator and creature are equal regarding essential impossibilities (like the conjunction of contradictions), it is not the case that everything beyond a creature's power is beyond the Creator's power. Therefore, using human incapacity as evidence for absolute impossibility is invalid.

Key Terms
مستحيل في ذاته
قدرة البشر
قدرة الخالق
المستحيلات الذاتية
الجمع بين النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is impossible for humans, it must be impossible for God.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this is false except for essential impossibilities (like contradictions). Human limitations do not imply divine limitations.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali argue about using human incapacity to create from nothing as evidence for absolute impossibility?
A) It is a valid proof because humans and God share the same limitations.
B) It is invalid because the Creator's power is not limited to what is possible for creatures.
C) It is valid only if the philosopher also proves that matter is eternal.
D) It is invalid because the philosophers do not believe in a Creator.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' argument conflates relative and absolute impossibility, and that only essential impossibilities apply to both human and divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to you, do you instinctively conclude that it is also impossible for God? Will you today consciously remind yourself that your limitations are not God's limitations, and trust Him to act in that area?
?
The philosophers shifted the subject from God's power to human power to avoid acknowledging divine omnipotence. Have you, in any area, been shifting the standard or making excuses to avoid submitting to a command of Allah? Identify one such area and repent, realigning to His power.
?
This lesson shows that logical impossibility is the only thing that limits God. What 'impossible' situation in your life have you been treating as impossible for God simply because you cannot solve it? Will you surrender it to Him today and expect His intervention?

The Problem of Specifying Matter's Dimensions
A dilemma on what determines matter's finite dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the problem of specifying matter's dimensions to challenge the philosopher's belief in an eternal universe with a divine agent.

Core Idea
If matter is eternal, its specific size lacks a coherent cause.

Ghazali presents a sharp dilemma for philosophers who believe both in an eternal material world and in a God who governs it. He asks: if matter was never created from nothing, what determines its exact dimensions? Either God determines them, or matter determines itself. But Ghazali argues that neither option is acceptable given the philosopher's own assumptions. This passage forces the reader to see a contradiction in holding both eternity of matter and divine governance.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ghazali's question
Read the passage and identify the core question: what specifies matter's size if it is eternal?
Notice that Ghazali assumes dimensions are finite, as the philosophers hold.
2
Consider the two possible causes
The passage presents two candidates: God or matter itself. Examine Ghazali's objection to each.
For God: He cannot add or subtract because creation from nothing and annihilation are impossible. For matter: it would have to cause its own specification, which seems absurd.
3
See the dilemma for theistic philosophers
Ghazali addresses those who affirm both a divine agent and the eternity of matter. Ask yourself how they could reconcile God's governance with matter self-determining its size.
This dilemma is meant to force a choice between eternal matter and divine agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا كانت المادة لم توجد من عدم فما الذي خصصها بما هي عليه من حجم ومقدار ؟ إن الأبعاد متناهية ، كما أثبته الفلاسفة ، فالمادة نتيجة لذلك - ذات حجم محدود ، ومقدار محدود . فما الذي جعلها على هذا القدر المحدود ، وعلى هذا الحجم المحدود ؟ هل الله هو الذي جعلها كذلك ؟ وكيف وليس في وسعه أن يضيف إليها شيئاً ؛ لأن الخلق من العدم مستحيل ... ولا في وسعه أن ينقص منها شيئاً ؛ لأن الموجود لا يصير إلى عدم ؟ أم المادة هي التي جعلت نفسها على هذا المقدار الخاص ، وذلك الحجم الخاص ؟ وكيف جعلت المادة نفسها كذلك ؟ وهل يلتزم هذا من يقولون : إن المادة لا توجد من عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ... إن بعض من يقول ذلك مؤمنون بوجود إله متصرف في الكون ، فهل يستطيع هؤلاء أن يوفقوا بين إيمانهم هذا ، وبين القول بأن المادة هي التي جعلت نفسها على هذا القدر الخاص ، وذلك الحجم الخاص ؟
Then, if matter did not exist from nothing, what specified it to be as it is in terms of size and quantity? Indeed, dimensions are finite, as the philosophers have established, so matter consequently is of a limited size and a limited quantity. So what made it to be of this particular limited amount, and this limited size? Is it God who made it so? But how, while He is not able to add anything to it, because creation from nothing is impossible... nor is He able to subtract anything from it, because what exists does not become nothing? Or is it matter itself that made itself to be of this particular quantity and that particular size? And how did matter make itself so? And is this commitment accepted by those who say: matter does not arise from nothing and does not become nothing... Some of those who say that are believers in a God who disposes in the universe. So can these people reconcile their faith with the claim that matter made itself to be of this particular amount and that particular size?
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage raises the problem of specification. Assuming matter is eternal (not created from nothing), Ghazali asks: what determines its specific finite dimensions? He considers two options: either God determines them, or matter determines them. But if God is unable to create ex nihilo and matter cannot be annihilated, then God cannot increase or decrease matter's size, so He cannot be the cause of its exact dimensions. Alternatively, if matter determines its own dimensions, that seems to attribute self-agency to matter. Ghazali then asks those who both affirm a divine agent and the eternity of matter: how can they reconcile God's governance with matter self-determining its size? The passage thus creates a dilemma for the philosopher who also believes in God.

Key Terms
تخصيص
حجم
مقدار
أبعاد متناهية
خلق من العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an eternal material world simply exists without needing a cause for its specific dimensions.
Correction: Ghazali insists that any specific determination requires a cause; randomness cannot account for particular finite measurements.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why cannot God be the one who specified matter's dimensions if matter is eternal?
A) Because God does not care about matter.
B) Because God cannot add to or subtract from matter since creation from nothing is impossible and existing things do not become nothing.
C) Because matter is too small for God to notice.
D) Because God is not powerful enough.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the specific finite dimensions of an eternal matter require a specifying cause, and neither God nor matter itself can provide it under the philosophers' own assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you ever assumed that the universe's specific size and structure just happened without a cause, how does this passage challenge that assumption? Would you be willing to accept that there must be a specifying cause beyond the material world, even if it means admitting a power that can create from nothing and annihilate? What would that change about how you view the world today?
?
Ghazali argues that if matter is eternal, God cannot be its specifier because He cannot add or subtract. Do you believe that God is truly sovereign over all things, including the ability to create and annihilate? Or do you inadvertently restrict His power by holding onto some old, unexamined assumption about the world? Examine your heart: is there any area of your life where you think God's hands are tied?
?
The philosophers believed in both eternal matter and a God who governs the universe. Ghazali says this is inconsistent. In your own life, are you clinging to contradictory beliefs—for example, believing in God's sovereignty yet living as if material circumstances have ultimate control? What is one such contradiction you need to resolve today, and what step will you take?

What Determines Matter's Specific Dimensions? Ghazali's Critique of Denying Creation from Nothing
Ghazali questions what specifies matter's size and criticizes the claim that creation from nothing is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali critiques the philosophers' claim that creation from nothing is impossible by questioning what specifies matter's size and accusing them of intellectual laziness.

Core Idea
Ghazali challenges the self-evidence of creation ex nihilo's impossibility by asking: what determines matter's specific dimensions?

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa presents a powerful critique of the philosophical position that denies creation from nothing. Ghazali does not merely argue against it; he first poses a dilemma about what specifies matter's size, then points out the inconsistency of those who believe in God yet deny His power to create or annihilate. Finally, he accuses the philosophers of underestimating the issue and encouraging intellectual laziness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's rhetorical questions sink in.
The passage is a series of questions building to a conclusion.
2
Understand the dilemma about matter's size
Ghazali asks: if matter is eternal, what determines its specific size? He presents two options: either God or matter itself. If God, but He cannot add or subtract (because the philosophers say creation and annihilation are impossible), then God's power is limited. If matter itself, how? This dilemma aims to show the absurdity of the philosophers' position.
3
Notice the contradiction for theists
Ghazali points out that some who deny creation from nothing still believe in a God who acts in the universe. He asks how they can reconcile this with the idea that matter determines its own specifications. This exposes a tension in their worldview.
4
Grasp Ghazali's final accusation
Ghazali concludes that the claim of self-evidence for the impossibility of creation ex nihilo is an underestimation and an enticement to laziness. He insists that these issues require thorough examination.
This is the key takeaway: the denial of creation from nothing is not self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا كانت المادة لم توجد من عدم فما الذي خصصها بما هي عليه من حجم ومقدار ؟ إن الأبعاد متناهية ، كما أثبته الفلاسفة ، فالمادة نتيجة لذلك - ذات حجم محدود ، ومقدار محدود . ― فما الذي جعلها على هذا القدر المحدود ، وعلى هذا الحجم المحدود ؟ هل الله هو الذي جعلها كذلك ؟ وليس في وسعه أن يضيف إليها شيئاً ؛ لأن الخلق من العدم مستحيل ... ولا في وسعه أن ينقص منها شيئاً ؛ لأن الموجود لا يصير إلى عدم ؟ أم المادة المادة هي التي جعلت نفسها على هذا المقدار الخاص ، وذلك الحجم الخاص ؟ وكيف جعلت المادة نفسها كذلك ؟ وهل يلتزم هذا من يقولون : إن المادة لا توجد من عدم ، ولا تصير إلى عدم ... إن بعض من يقول ذلك مؤمنون بوجود إله متصرف في الكون ، فهل يستطيع هؤلاء أن يوفقوا بين إيمانهم هذا ، وبين القول بأن المادة هي التي جعلت نفسها على هذا القدر الخاص ، وذلك الحجم الخاص ؟ كل هذا يجعلنى أقول : إن دعوى بداهة : عدم إمكان إيجاد الشيء من عدم • وعدم إمكان صيرورة الشيء إلى عدم استهانة بشأن هاتين المسألتين ، وإغراء للعقل بالكسل عن فحصهما فحصاً يفضي إلى التأكد من حقيقة الأمر فيهما .
Then if matter did not come into existence from non-existence, what specified it with the size and amount that it has? Indeed, dimensions are finite, as the philosophers have established, so matter as a result is of limited volume and limited quantity. So what made it be of this limited measure and this limited volume? Is it God who made it thus? But He is not able to add anything to it, because creation from non-existence is impossible... nor is He able to decrease anything from it, because what exists does not become non-existence. Or is matter itself that made itself this particular measure and this particular volume? And how did matter make itself thus? And do those who say that matter does not come from non-existence nor does it become non-existence commit to this? Indeed, some who say that believe in a God who disposes in the universe, so can these people reconcile between this faith of theirs and the statement that matter made itself this particular measure and this particular volume? All this makes me say: The claim of the self-evidence of the impossibility of bringing something into existence from non-existence and the impossibility of something becoming non-existence is an underestimation of the status of these two issues, and an enticement of the intellect to laziness from examining them thoroughly, an examination that leads to ascertaining the truth of the matter concerning them.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a series of questions and a critique. It begins by stating the philosophers' position that matter does not come from nothing and that dimensions are finite. Ghazali then asks what determines matter's specific size and volume. He poses a dilemma: either God determined it or matter determined itself. If God determined it, but God cannot add or subtract because creation from nothing is impossible and what exists cannot become nothing (as the philosophers claim), then God's power is limited. If matter determined itself, how? He then points out a contradiction: some who deny creation from nothing still believe in a God who acts in the universe; they must reconcile this with the view that matter determines its own specifications. Ghazali concludes that the claim that creation from nothing is self-evidently impossible is an underestimation and an enticement to intellectual laziness. He insists that both issues—creation from nothing and annihilation to nothing—require thorough examination.

عدم
إيجاد
فناء
بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of creation from nothing is self-evident and needs no examination.
Correction: Ghazali argues that this claim is not self-evident; it is an underestimation that discourages thorough inquiry.

Checkpoint
Ghazali argues that the claim that creating something from nothing is impossible is:
A) A self-evident truth that needs no proof.
B) An underestimation that leads the intellect to laziness.
C) A proven fact by the philosophers.
D) A statement that only naturalists accept.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that denying creation from nothing leads to unsolvable dilemmas and accuses philosophers of intellectual laziness for claiming self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold about God's power that you have never really examined, assuming they are self-evident? What might you discover if you subjected them to the same rigorous questioning Ghazali applies to the philosophers?
?
Ghazali accuses the philosophers of intellectual laziness—enticing the intellect to be lazy. Where in your own spiritual life have you accepted a position without careful thought because it seemed obvious? Will you commit to re-examining one such assumption this week?
?
Can you honestly say you trust that God has absolute power over all things, including the ability to create from nothing and to annihilate? Or do you unconsciously limit His power as the philosophers did? How does that show up in your daily trust in Him?

Galen's Argument for the World's Eternity and Ghazali's Rational Critique
Ghazali presents Galen's argument for the world's eternity and his own rational critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's empirical argument for the eternity of the world based on the sun's lack of change, and Ghazali's rational critique that subordinates natural science to reason.

Core Idea
The sun's constancy does not prove eternity; reason must oversee observation.

Galen, a renowned physician and philosopher, argued that the world is eternal because the sun has remained unchanged for thousands of years—no wilting, no atrophy. He took this as evidence that the world does not decay and is therefore eternal. However, al-Ghazali does not engage with Galen's astronomical data. Instead, he adopts a purely rational stance, asserting that reason must correct and direct natural scientific inquiries, not be led by them. This lesson presents Galen's argument and Ghazali's methodological approach.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting Galen's reasoning: the sun's lack of change over time implies it will not perish.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Notice that Ghazali does not counter with empirical evidence. Instead, he positions himself as a rational overseer of natural science, correcting its methods.
3
Reflect on the epistemological stance
Consider why Ghazali would set aside empirical data for a purely rational critique. How does this reflect his overall approach in the Tahafut?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلقد ذهب جالينوس - مع من ذهبوا - إلى أن العالم أبدى ، وقدم بين يدى دعواه هذه ، قوله : ) إن فساد العالم يكون بتحوله من حال إلى حال ، وبانتقاله من شأن إلى شأن . ولكنا نرى الشمس على حالها التي كانت عليها منذ آلاف السنين، لم يعتورها ذبول ، و لم يطرأ عليها ضمور 13. ومعنى هذا أنها لن تفنى ، إذ لو كانت في طريقها إلى الفناء ، لوجب تعرضها، خلال آلاف السنين التى مرت بها ، إلى التحلل والذبول . أما وهى لم تذبل ولم تتحلل ، خلال هذه الآماد المديدة ، فليس لذلك إلا تفسير واحد ، هو أنها ثابتة لا تفنى ، باقية لا تزول (۲)) هذا ما يقوله « جالينوس » بصدد نظرية « أبدية العالم » ولقد عول ( جالينوس » ، فى حكمه بعدم تعرض الشمس للذبول ، خلال عمرها الطويل ، على آلات الرصد التي كانت معروفة لعهده . ولكن الغزالى لم يعول على الأرصاد الجوية ، ولم يقف من المسألة موقفاً يستمد معلوماته عنها ، من العلم الطبيعي ، وإنما وقف منها موقفاً عقليا صرفاً ، وقف منها موقف المهيمن على البحوث الطبيعية يقومها ويسدد خطاها . قال الغزالي : [ الاعتراض عليه من وجوه : (۱) هذا ليس ترجمة حرفية لعبارة جالينوس ، ولكنها رواية بالمعنى - انظر تهافت الفلاسفة : مسألة في إبطال قولهم في أبدية العالم والزمان والحركة
For Galen went - along with those who went - to the view that the world is eternal, and he presented before this claim of his his statement: 'The corruption of the world is through its transformation from state to state and its transition from condition to condition. But we see the sun in the same state it was in thousands of years ago; no wilting has affected it, and no atrophy has occurred to it. And this means that it will not perish, for if it were on the path to perdition, it would have been necessary for it, during the thousands of years that have passed, to undergo dissolution and wilting. But since it has not wilted nor dissolved over these immense durations, there is no explanation for that except one: that it is stable, not perishing, abiding, not vanishing.' This is what Galen says regarding the theory of 'the eternity of the world'. And Galen relied, in his judgment that the sun had not undergone wilting over its long life, on the astronomical instruments that were known in his time. But al-Ghazali did not rely on meteorological observations, nor did he take a stance on the issue deriving his information from natural science; rather, he took a purely rational stance, a stance of one who oversees natural researches, correcting them and directing their steps. Al-Ghazali said: 'The objection to it is from several aspects:' (1) This is not a literal translation of Galen's phrase, but a paraphrase - see Tahafut al-Falasifa: the discussion on refuting their statement on the eternity of the world, time, and motion.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's empirical argument for the eternity of the world, followed by Ghazali's approach. Galen argues that the sun has remained unchanged for millennia, implying it will not perish, and since perishing would require change, the sun's stability proves its eternity. He relies on astronomical observations. Ghazali, however, does not base his response on natural science or empirical data. Instead, he adopts a purely rational position, supervising natural science from above, correcting it. He begins his objection (which continues later) by noting multiple aspects. The footnote clarifies that the quotation from Galen is not verbatim but a paraphrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali rejects all empirical evidence outright.
Correction: Ghazali does not reject empirical evidence per se; he argues that rational principles must govern how we interpret and correct natural scientific observations.

Checkpoint
On what basis did Galen argue that the sun will not perish?
A) The sun's heat and light are eternal.
B) The sun has not wilted or atrophied over thousands of years.
C) The philosophers have proven it.
D) The sun's motion is circular.

Key Takeaway
Galen's empirical argument for the world's eternity is based on the sun's lack of change, but Ghazali counters by asserting that rational critique must guide, not follow, natural science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have undoubtedly trusted your eyes many times. But when has something in your life appeared unchanged while inwardly decaying? The sun's stability fooled Galen. What in your faith, character, or relationships appears constant but might be eroding? Identify one area today and commit to examining it with rational honesty, not just comfortable observation.
?
Galen built an entire worldview on the sun's lack of change. Think of a belief you hold about yourself—your habits, your worth, your future—that you have never questioned because 'it has always been this way'. What if that belief is as deceptive as the sun's apparent permanence? Will you submit it to the same rational critique Ghazali applied? Name that belief and set a time this week to genuinely challenge it.
?
Al-Ghazali refused to let empirical evidence have the final say; he insisted on rational oversight. What is one decision you are making right now based solely on what you see and feel? How would your life shift if you took a 'purely rational stance' and evaluated it from a higher perspective? Commit to one concrete step to apply reason over raw observation in that decision today.

The First Objection: Refuting Customary Limits
Ghazali presents the first metaphysical thrust against Galen's empirical limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the first metaphysical thrust to argue that reason must not be constrained by habitual observation.

Core Idea
Habit is not proof; reason must transcend custom.

In this passage, Ghazali begins his critique of the natural philosopher Galen by challenging the assumption that the sun cannot corrupt. He uses an analogy of a ruby to show that imperceptible change does not mean no change. The first objection is a metaphysical thrust that frees thought from being bound by familiarity and habit, asserting that reason must not be limited by what we have merely observed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Notice the analogy of the sun and the ruby and the strong claim about habit.
The passage contains a footnote marker but it doesn't affect the argument.
2
Understand the analogy
The sun is enormous (170 times Earth), so a decrease like mountains would be imperceptible. Similarly, a ruby placed for a hundred years shows no perceptible loss. The point is that lack of observed decay does not prove decay hasn't occurred.
3
Identify the first thrust
The objection elevates thought from being narrow and stagnant. It states that no rational person can claim that because we have only observed withering, it must be the only path to corruption. That claim is made only by a man of habit, not a free-thinking intellect.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ghazali is arguing that empirical observation alone cannot establish necessity. The absence of observed alternatives does not prove impossibility. This is a key metaphysical stance against purely empirical philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشمس التي يقال : إنها كالأرض مائة وسبعين مرة ، أو ما يقرب منه ، لو نقص منها مقدار جبال مثلا ، لكان لا يتبين للحس (١) فلعلها في الذبول ، وإلى الآن قد نقص مقدار جبال فأكثر، والحس لا يقدر على أن يدرك ذلك ؛ لأن تقديره في علم المناظر ، لا يعرف إلا بالتقريب. وهذا كما أن الياقوت والذهب مركبان من العناصر عندهم ، وهي قابلة للفساد تم لو وضعت ياقوتة مائة سنة ، لم يكن نقصانها محسوساً فلعل نسبة ما ينقص من الشمس في مدة تاريخ الأرصاد ، كنسبة ما ينقص من الياقوتة ، في مائة سنة وذلك لا يظهر للحس . فدل أن دليله في غاية الفساد ] * إنهما طعنتان يصوبهما رجل الميتافيزيقا ، رجل العقل الصرف، والنظر المجرد ، نحو رجل الفيزيقا ، رجل الأرصاد ، والأبحاث المادية . طعنتان قاتلتان : أما أولاهما : فتسمو بالفكر عن أن يضيق ويجمد ، ويلتزم حدود الإلف والعادة ، لا يبرحهما ، ولا يتخلص من أثرهما نعم . إنه لا يستطيع عاقل أن يزعم أنه إذا كنا لم نصادف في حياتنا طريقاً لفساد الكائنات ، سوى الذبول ، فلابد أن يكون الطريق الوحيد إلى الفساد ، هو الذبول . من ذلك ؟ اللهم لا أحد ، إلا رجل الألف والعادة ، لا رجل العقل المفكر ، والذهن المتحرر
And the sun, which is said to be like the earth one hundred and seventy times, or close to that, if a quantity like mountains were to decrease from it, for example, it would not be perceptible to the senses. Perhaps it is in a state of withering (ذبول), and until now a quantity like mountains or more may have decreased, and the senses cannot perceive that; because its estimation in the science of perspective (علم المناظر) is only known approximately. This is like the ruby and gold, which according to them are composed of elements, and are susceptible to corruption. If a ruby were placed for a hundred years, its decrease would not be perceptible. So perhaps the ratio of what decreases from the sun over the period of recorded observations is like the ratio of what decreases from the ruby in a hundred years, and that does not appear to the senses. Thus his argument is extremely corrupt. * These are two thrusts directed by the man of metaphysics, the man of pure reason and abstract reflection, towards the man of physics, the man of observations and material research. Two deadly thrusts: As for the first one, it elevates thought from being narrow and stagnant, and adhering to the limits of familiarity and habit, not moving beyond them nor freeing itself from their influence. Yes. No rational person can claim that if we have not encountered in our lives any path for the corruption of things except withering, then the only path to corruption must be withering. Who would claim that? No one, except a man of familiarity and habit, not a man of reflective reason and a liberated mind.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against Galen's denial of the sun's corruption. It begins with an illustrative example: the sun is enormous (170 times the earth), so a decrease as large as mountains would be imperceptible to the senses. Similarly, a ruby placed for a hundred years shows no perceptible loss. The point is that the absence of observed decay does not prove it hasn't occurred; it may be too gradual to detect. The author then characterizes this as a metaphysical thrust against physical scientists: the first objection liberates thought from being bound by custom and habit. No rational person can claim that because we have only observed one mode of corruption (withering/ذبول), it must be the only mode. That claim is only made by someone enslaved to familiarity, not by a free-thinking intellect.

Key Terms
ذبول
العقل الصرف
الإلف والعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we have only observed one mode of corruption (withering), then that must be the only possible mode.
Correction: The passage argues that this is an assumption based on habit, not logical necessity. Reason should not be bound by familiar patterns; absence of observed alternatives does not prove impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what mistake does Galen make regarding the corruption of the sun?
A) He relies on metaphysical proofs.
B) He assumes that because we have only observed withering, it is the only possible way for the sun to corrupt.
C) He denies that the sun can corrupt at all.
D) He uses excessive reason.

Key Takeaway
The absence of observed decay does not prove impossibility; reason must not be confined by habitual patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you insisted that something is impossible simply because you have never witnessed it? How does Ghazali's analogy of the sun and the ruby challenge that assumption, and what will you do this week to test it?
?
When have you let habit — the comfort of the familiar — close your mind to a truth that reason could have grasped? What would it cost you to break free of that habit, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
This passage calls you to be a person of pure reason, not of habit. Where in your faith or daily practice are you following custom blindly rather than thinking critically? Will you change that today, and what is the first step you will take?

The Second Objection: Challenging Galen's Certainty
Presents the second objection: granting the possibility of withering and questioning Galen's methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses reason to question empirical certainty, and how he employs experience itself to demonstrate the possibility of the sun's withering.

Core Idea
Reason can use experience to challenge false certainties.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces his second objection against Galen's denial of the sun's possible withering. Rather than claiming direct observation, Ghazali questions the very methods Galen used to conclude that withering has never occurred. He shows that reason can overturn empirical assumptions, even using new empirical evidence like solar explosions to illustrate his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the tone and the structure of the argument.
The passage begins by supposing possibility, then attacks Galen's methods, then uses experience to support reason.
2
Identify Ghazali's main strategy
Ghazali does not claim to have observed the withering. Instead, he questions the validity of Galen's methods. Find where he says 'يشكك جالينوس فى وسائل الجزم'.
This is key: he casts doubt on the means of certainty.
3
Reflect on the role of reason and experience
The passage states 'وينتصر العقل على التجربة بوساطة التجربة نفسها'. How does the example of solar explosions support this? Consider what Galen would have concluded if he had seen them.
The passage warns against relying solely on past experience.

وأما ثانيتهما ; فتفترض جواز حصول الذبول الذي ينكره جالينوس . وتتهم الوسائل التي عول عليها ) جالينوس ( فى الجزم بعدم حصوله . إنها حجة عقلية أيضاً ، تلك التى يتذرع بها الغزالى . إنه يفترض جواز الحصول ، ولا يدعى أنه قد توصل إلى إدراك الحصول بالفعل ، بآلات تيسرت له ولم تتيسر لجالينوس . إنه يشكك جالينوس فى وسائل الجزم بعدم الحصول ومرة أخرى ينتصر العقل على التجربة ، وينتصر العقل على التجربة بوساطة التجربة نفسها . فها هي ذى المراصد تطالعنا بين وقت وآخر ، باكتشاف حدوث انفجارات شمسية ، انفجارات تحدث بقعاً وتغيرات على سطحها ، تزيد مساحتها على مساحة سطح الأرض عشرين مرة . ولا تقتصر المراصد في إثبات حدوث هذه الانفجارات ، على مشاهدة مواضع الانفجارات ، بل تؤكده ، بما ينشأ عن الانفجارات من تيارات هوائية وموجات كهربائية ، تؤثر فى أوضاع الحياة على سطح الأرض فلو طالع جالينوس هذه الانفجارات وآثارها ، لم ينكر أن الشمس تتعرض للذبول . . . 6 يا لله ! ! ما أعظم ما أعطيتنا من عقل ، وما أجل ما وهبتنا من فكر إنهما شعلتان من نورك ومدد من قدرتك ، لا يضل من يهتدى بهما، ولا يغلب من يلوذ بحصنهما . فها هو ذا الغزالى ينتصر على جالينوس ، لسبب واحد ، هو : أن الغزالي تذرع بالعقل ، والنظر السديد ؛ وأن جالينوس ، استمسك بالمألوف ، وركن إليه ، وجمد عليه . ألا فليعتبر بهذا ، أوائك الذين يحمدون على التجارب ، ويتخذون منها وحدها سبيلا للعلم والمعرفة ، و يرون أن الوجود هو ما جربوه ، ولا شيء غير ما جربوه .
As for the second one, it supposes the possibility of the occurrence of the withering (ذبول) which Galen denies. And it accuses the means upon which Galen relied for certainty of its non-occurrence. It is also a rational argument, that which Ghazali uses. He supposes the possibility of occurrence, and does not claim that he has reached actual perception of occurrence, by instruments that became available to him and were not available to Galen. He casts doubt on Galen's means of certainty of non-occurrence. And again reason triumphs over experience, and reason triumphs over experience by means of experience itself. For here are the observatories informing us from time to time of the discovery of solar explosions, explosions that produce spots and changes on its surface, whose area exceeds the area of the earth's surface twenty times. And the observatories do not limit themselves to observing the locations of the explosions to prove their occurrence, but confirm it by what arises from the explosions of air currents and electric waves that affect the conditions of life on the earth's surface. So if Galen had seen these explosions and their effects, he would not have denied that the sun undergoes withering. . . O God! How great is the mind You have given us, and how magnificent the thought You have bestowed upon us! They are two flames from Your light and an extension of Your power; he who is guided by them does not stray, and he who seeks refuge in their fortress is not overcome. So here is Ghazali triumphing over Galen for one reason: that Ghazali employed reason and sound reflection, while Galen clung to the familiar, leaned upon it, and froze upon it. So let those who rely solely on experiments, and take them alone as the path to knowledge, and see that existence is only what they have experienced and nothing else, take heed from this.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against Galen. It assumes the possibility of the withering that Galen denies, and attacks the reliability of Galen's methods for concluding it has not occurred. The author notes that Ghazali does not claim to have actually observed the withering, but only to show its possibility. He casts doubt on Galen's certainty. The passage then declares that reason triumphs over experience, and even does so through experience itself: modern observatories have discovered solar explosions and their effects, which would have changed Galen's mind. The concluding part praises reason as a divine gift and states that Ghazali's victory over Galen stems from Ghazali's use of reason and sound reflection, while Galen clung to the familiar and became stagnant. The final call is a warning against those who limit knowledge to empirical experience alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reason and experience are necessarily opposed.
Correction: The passage shows that reason can use experience itself to overcome limitations of habit and false certainty.

Checkpoint
How does the second objection challenge Galen's denial of the sun's withering?
A) By claiming to have observed the withering directly.
B) By questioning the methods Galen used to be certain that it does not happen.
C) By relying solely on experience.
D) By accepting Galen's premise.

Key Takeaway
Reason, not habit, should guide our judgment about the possibilities of nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one belief you hold about the world that you have never questioned because 'it has always been that way'? How might your limited experience be deceiving you, just as Galen's experience deceived him about the sun?
?
Ghazali used reason to challenge Galen's certainty. When was the last time you used reason to examine a comfortable assumption about your faith or life? What held you back?
?
The passage says 'reason triumphs over experience by means of experience itself.' How can you actively use new experiences to correct your own assumptions, rather than ignoring them because they don't fit your expectations?

Ibn Sina's Causal Argument for the World's Pre-Eternity
Ibn Sina's causal argument for the pre-eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's causal argument that the world must be pre-eternal because its cause (the Necessary Existent) is eternal and unchanging.

Core Idea
An eternal cause with an unchanging state necessitates an eternal effect.

Ibn Sina presents a purely rational argument for the world's pre-eternity based on the nature of causality. He argues that the existence of an effect depends on the cause being in a specific state (e.g., nature or will). If that state is eternal and unchanging, the effect must be eternal. This sets up Ghazali's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's causal argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then consult the translation. Notice the key terms: cause, effect, state, nature, will.
2
Understand the causal relation
Identify the two conditions: existence of effect depends on cause being in a specific state; non-existence depends on cause not being in that state.
The cause's state determines the effect's existence.
3
Apply to the First Cause
Since the First Cause (Necessary Existent) is eternal and unchanging, its state is eternal. Therefore, its effect (the world) must be eternal.
This is the core of Ibn Sina's proof.

1
مثلا
وجود المعلول متعلق بالعلة من حيث هي على الحال التي بها تكون علة : من طبيعة ، أو إرادة ، أو غير ذلك من أمور يحتاج إلى أن تكون من خارج ، ولها مدخل فى تتميم كون العلة علة بالفعل
وعدم المعلول متعلق بعدم كون العلة على الحال التي هي بها علة بالفعل سواء كانت ذاتها موجودة لا على تلك الحال ، أو لم تكن موجودة أصلاً فإذا لم يكن شيء معوقاً من خارج ، وكان الفاعل بذاته موجوداً ، ولكنه ليس لذاته علة ، توقف وجود المعلول على وجود الحالة المذكورة ، فإذا وجدت كانت طبيعة ، - أو إرادة جازمة ، أو غير ذلك - وجب وجود المعلول ؛ وإن لم توجد ، وجب عدمه
·
وأيهما فرض أبداً ، كان ما بإزائه أبداً ، أو وقتاً ما ، كان ما بإزائه وقتاً ما . و إذا جاز أن يكون شيء متشابه الحال في كل شيء ، وله معلول ، لم يبعد أن يجب عنه سرمداً ، فإذا لم يسم هذا مفعولاً - بسبب بسبب أنه لم يتقدمه عدم زمانی - فلا مضايقة فى الأسماء بعد ظهور المعنى (١) ]
ويقول :
فإن كان الداعى إلى تعطيل واجب الوجود عن إفاضة الخير والجود ،
هو كون المعلول مسبوق العدم لا محالة ، فهذا الداعي ضعيف قد انكشف
لذوى الإنصاف ضعفه (۲)]

وأما كون المعلول ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، واجب الوجود بغيره ، فليس
يناقض كونه دائم الوجود بغيره ، كما نبهت عليه (١)
安
And within the scope of this meaning falls the work that al-Ghazali undertook in his book At-Tahāfut. For, if Ibn Sīnā relied on reason and was inspired by it, so also did al-Ghazali. And if Ibn Sīnā follows a purely rational path by which he demonstrates the necessity of the world being pre-eternal, saying:
1
For example: The existence of the effect is connected to the cause in respect of the state in which it is a cause: whether by nature, will, or other matters that need to be external and have a role in completing the cause being an actual cause. And the non-existence of the effect is connected to the cause not being in the state in which it is an actual cause, whether its essence exists not in that state or does not exist at all. So if there is no impediment from outside, and the agent exists by its essence but its essence is not a cause, the existence of the effect depends on the existence of the mentioned state. When that state exists—whether it be nature, decisive will, or something else—the existence of the effect is necessary; and if it does not exist, its non-existence is necessary.
And whichever is assumed eternally, that which corresponds to it is eternal; or for a time, that which corresponds to it is for a time. And if it is possible for something to be uniform in every respect and have an effect, it is not unlikely that [the effect] should be necessitated from it perpetually. So if this is not called 'effected' because no temporal non-existence preceded it, there is no quarrel about names after the meaning is clear (1).
And he says: If the motive for preventing the Necessary Existent from overflowing with goodness and bounty is that the effect must be preceded by non-existence, then this motive is weak—its weakness has been disclosed to those who are fair (2).
·
(1) Chapter Eight of the Fifth Pattern of Al-Ishārāt wa-t-Tanbīhāt, Dār al-Ma‘ārif edition. (2) Chapter Twelve of the Fifth Pattern of Al-Ishārāt wa-t-Tanbīhāt, Dār al-Ma‘ārif edition.
As for the effect being possible in itself and necessary through another, that does not contradict its being perpetual through another, as I have pointed out (1).
[stray character]
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage introduces Ibn Sina's rational arguments for the world's pre-eternity, which Ghazali will later refute. The editor first notes that Ghazali, like Ibn Sina, relies on reason. Then Ibn Sina's argument is presented in three parts. The first part (quoting from his Ishārāt) explains the causal relation: The existence of an effect depends on the cause being in a specific state (e.g., nature or will). If the cause is in that state eternally, the effect is eternal; if temporally, the effect is temporal. Since the First Cause is eternal and unchangeable, its effect (the world) must be eternal. The second part argues that if the world must be preceded by non-existence, that motive is weak; the Necessary Existent's generosity is constant. The third part clarifies that the world's possibility in itself does not contradict its perpetual existence through another. Together, this forms Ibn Sina's rationalist case for the world's eternity.

المعلول
الطبيعة

قدم العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Sina rejects the possibility of a temporal beginning for the world.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that the world is necessarily pre-eternal given an eternal cause; he does not consider a temporal creation possible.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what determines whether an effect is eternal or temporal?
A) The state of the cause being uniform or changing
B) The will of the cause
C) The nature of the effect
D) The absence of impediments

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's causal argument concludes that an eternal and unchanging cause must produce an eternal effect, implying the world's pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a rational proof for the world's beginninglessness. If that is true, what does that mean for your belief that the world was created in time? Does this challenge your faith, or do you have a response? Sit with that discomfort before moving on.
?
Ibn Sina's argument hinges on the cause being 'in a certain state'. Consider your own relationship with God: are you expecting God to act based on your understanding of His state? How does this argument expose the danger of measuring the Divine by human standards?
?
The passage concludes that if there is no impediment, the effect must be eternal. In your own life, are there things you believe God 'must do' because you assume no impediment? How might you be limiting God's will with your own logic?

Ibn Sina's Defense of Perpetual Emanation
Ibn Sina's additional arguments for the world's pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's second and third arguments that the Necessary Existent's generosity is constant and that possibility in itself does not preclude eternal existence through another.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent's perfection requires constant giving, and possibility is not a barrier to perpetuity.

Ibn Sina reinforces his proof by addressing potential objections. He argues that denying the world's pre-eternity would imply a defect in the Necessary Existent's generosity. He also clarifies that something may be possible in itself yet necessary through another, allowing it to be perpetual. These arguments strengthen the rational case for the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the two additional arguments
Read the second quote about the motive for preventing emanation, then the third about possibility and perpetuity.
2
Analyze the second argument
Ibn Sina says that if the only obstacle to emanation is that the effect must be preceded by non-existence, this is a weak motive. The Necessary Existent's goodness demands constant giving.
3
Analyze the third argument
The world is possible in itself but necessary through Allah. This does not contradict its perpetual existence; it depends on its cause being eternal.
The world's possibility is compatible with its eternity.

According to Ibn Sina, why is the motive for preventing the Necessary Existent from overflowing with goodness weak?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's perpetual generosity requires constant emanation of existence, why do you still feel that His mercy is intermittent—tied to your good deeds rather than His nature? What would it mean for your daily life to truly believe that His giving never stops, even when you are in sin? Will you pause right now to thank Him for sustaining you in this moment, despite your shortcomings?
?
Ibn Sina argues that possibility is not a barrier to perpetuity. What is one area of your life—repentance, healing, or a broken relationship—that you have considered 'impossible' because it hasn't happened yet? If the world can be both possible and eternal, what might be possible for you if you stopped using time as an excuse? What step will you take today to act on that possibility?
?
You are 'necessary through another'—you exist only because Allah holds you in existence. How does that truth change the way you view your own plans and ambitions? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or as if every breath depends on Him? What will you do in the next hour to remind yourself that you are not your own cause?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Objection and answer: possibility of a pre-eternal will causing a temporal event
Ghazali presents and refutes the objection that a temporal event cannot stem from a pre-eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's challenge to the objection that a temporal event cannot stem from a pre-eternal will, and his demand for a logical proof.

Core Idea
Ghazali shifts the burden of proof: show me the syllogism or admit it's not self-evident.

Ghazali presents an objection from his opponents: if a cause is fully ready, its effect must happen immediately—so a pre-eternal will cannot cause a temporal event after delay. Then he gives his answer, questioning how the opponents know this impossibility. This lesson walks through the objection and Ghazali's clever rejoinder, which forces the opponent to produce a logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from the opponent: why they think a pre-eternal will cannot cause a temporal event. Note the reasoning about cause and effect.
The objector uses the principle that a fully prepared cause must give its effect immediately.
2
Read Ghazali's answer
Now read Ghazali's response. He challenges the opponent to specify how they know the impossibility: by immediate rational necessity or by a syllogism?
He then points out that if it were necessary, all rational people would agree, but they don't.
3
Understand the burden of proof
Ghazali demands that if the opponent claims syllogistic knowledge, they must produce the middle term. Otherwise, the claim is unsubstantiated.
This shifts the debate to requiring a valid logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل هذا محال بين الإحالة ؛ لأن الحادث موجب ومسبب ، وكما يستحيل حادث بغير سبب وموجب ، يستحيل وجود موجب قد تم بشرائط إيجابه وأركانه وأسبابه ، حتى لم يبق شيء منتظر ألبتة ، ثم يتأخر الموجب ؛ بل وجود الموجب عند تحقق الموجيب بتمام شروطه ، ضرورى ، وتأخره محال.

والجواب أن يقال : استحالة إرادة قديمة متعلقة بأحداث شيء - أي شيء كان ـ تعرفونه بضرورة العقل أو نظره ؟ وعلى لغتكم في المنطق ، تعرفون الالتقاء بين هذين الحدين ، بحد أوسط، أو من غير حد أوسط ؟ فإن ادعيتم حدا أوسط - - وهو الطريق النظرى - فلا بد من إظهاره ، و إن ادعيتم معرفة ذلك ضرورة ، فكيف لم يشارككم فى معرفته مخالفوكم ، والفرقة المتعقدة لحدوث العالم بإرادة قديمة لا يحصرها بلد ولا يحصيها عدد ، ولا شك في أنهم لا يكابرون العقول عناداً مع المعرفة ، فلابد من إقامة برهان - على شرط المنطق – يدل على
استحالة ذلك
For if it is said: This is clearly impossible, because the temporal event is an effect and a caused thing; and just as a temporal event without a cause and an effecter is impossible, so also is it impossible for an effecter that has fulfilled all the conditions, pillars, and causes of its effecting—such that nothing is left to wait for—to then delay the effect. Rather, the existence of the effecter upon the realization of the effect with all its conditions is necessary, and its delay is impossible.
And the answer is to say: Do you know the impossibility of a pre-eternal will being connected to the origination of something—anything—by necessity of reason or by its reflection? And according to your terminology in logic, do you know the conjunction between these two terms through a middle term or without a middle term? If you claim a middle term—which is the theoretical path—you must produce it. And if you claim knowledge of that by necessity, then why have your opponents, who are the faction that believes in the temporal creation of the world by a pre-eternal will—a faction that no country can contain nor number count—not shared in that knowledge? And there is no doubt that they do not stubbornly oppose reason out of obstinacy while knowing. Therefore, a proof must be established—according to the conditions of logic—that demonstrates the impossibility of that.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to Ghazali's model and his response. The objector argues that a temporal event must have a cause, and if the cause is fully prepared, the effect must follow immediately; otherwise delay is impossible. Therefore, a pre-eternal will that causes a temporal event after a period of non-action would involve delay, which is absurd. In his answer, Ghazali challenges the objector to explain how they know that such a scenario is impossible. He asks whether they know it through immediate rational necessity or through a syllogism. If through a syllogism, they must produce the middle term. If through necessity, then why do the multitudes who believe in creation by a pre-eternal will not share that necessity? He argues that it is not self-evident, so a proper logical proof is required. This shifts the burden of proof onto the opponents.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة (pre-eternal will)
حادث (temporal event)
موجب (effecter, cause)
مسبب (caused)
برهان (proof)
ضرورة العقل (necessity of reason)
حد أوسط (middle term)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies causality altogether.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny causality; he questions the necessary connection between cause and effect in this specific context, demanding a logical proof for the impossibility of a pre-eternal will causing a temporal event.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main challenge to the objection that a pre-eternal will cannot cause a temporal event?
A) He demands a logical proof for the impossibility
B) He asserts it is possible by divine omnipotence
C) He denies the principle of causality
D) He argues that the will is temporal

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shifts the burden of proof: the opponent must either produce a syllogism or admit that the impossibility is not self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an objection to your faith that sounds logical, do you assume it must be correct, or do you first ask: 'Is this based on necessity or assumption?' This lesson demands you hold every claim to the same standard. Will you commit to examining the basis of objections before accepting them?
?
Ghazali challenges the objector to produce a proof. In your own life, how often do you accept beliefs about Allah without checking if they are truly necessary or just inherited? What is one belief you have held that you have never examined? Will you today test it against a logical standard?
?
You have seen that a pre-eternal will causing temporal events is not proven impossible. How does this affect your certainty in the Qur'anic teaching that Allah creates as He wills? More importantly, does this intellectual confirmation move your heart to deeper trust in Allah's power? What will you do this week to strengthen that trust beyond mere intellectual agreement?
