The Absolute Impossibility and the Notice Against Purpose
Ghazali delivers the absolute conclusion and a warning against purposive explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's final conclusion that an event from the eternal without change is absolutely impossible, and his critique of ascribing purpose to divine action.

Core Idea
Any change in the eternal is as impossible as the original event, and God's acts have no 'why'.

Having shown the infinite regress problem, Ghazali now delivers his decisive verdict. He declares that it is absolutely impossible for a new event to proceed from the eternal without some change. He then adds a sharp notice rejecting the philosophers' attempts to explain divine action through purpose or beauty.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the final part of Ghazali's argument. Notice the shift from hypothetical reasoning to a definitive statement.
The phrase 'fithan qad tahaqqaqa bil-qawl al-mutlaq' indicates an absolute conclusion.
2
Understand the absolute impossibility
Ghazali states that the emanation of an event from the eternal without change in power, instrument, time, purpose, or nature is impossible. He also says that positing a change of state is equally impossible because the same problem applies to that change.
This covers all possible ways the philosophers might try to escape.
3
Analyze the notice (tanbih)
Ghazali condemns the idea that high beings act for the sake of what is below them for beauty, or that the First Truth acts for a purpose. He calls these explanations ugly and impossible.
This reinforces that divine action has no external motive.
4
Grasp the final conditional
Ghazali concludes: if the world exists and its origination is impossible, then its eternity is necessarily established. But note: this is the philosophers' own conclusion, which Ghazali has shown to be based on flawed reasoning.
Ghazali is not admitting eternity; he is showing the logical consequence of their premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد أن لم يكن مريداً، فتكون قد حدثت الإرادة، وحدوثه في ذاته محال، لأنه ليس محل الحوادث، وحدوثه لا في ذاته لا يجعله مريداً. ولنترك النظر في محل حدوثه، أليس الإشكال قائماً في أصل حدوثه؟ وأنه من؟ أين حدث؟ ولم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبله؟ أحدث الآن لا من جهة الله؟ فإن جاز حادث من غير محدث، فليكن العالم حادثاً لا صانع له، وإلا فأي فرق بين حادث وحادث؟ وإن حدث بإحداث الله، فلم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبل؟ ألعدم آلة، أو قدرة، أو غرض، أو طبيعة. فلما أن تبدل ذلك بالوجود حدث؟ عاد الإشكال بعينه. أو لعدم الإرادة؟ فتفتقر الإرادة إلى إرادة وكذا الإرادة الأولى، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية. فإذن قد تحقق بالقول المطلق أن صدور الحادث من القديم من غير تغير أمر من القديم في قدرة أو آلة أو وقت أو غرض أو طبع محال، وتقدير تغير حال محال، لأن الكلام في ذلك التغير الحادث كالكلام في غيره. تنبيه: فما أقبح ما يقال من أن الأمور العالية تحاول أن تفعل شيئاً لما تحتها لأن ذلك أحسن بها، ولتكون فعالة للجميل، أو أن الأول الحق يفعل شيئاً لأجل شيء، وأن لفعله لمية. والكل محال، ومهما كان العالم موجوداً واستحال حدوثه، ثبت قدمه لا محالة.
After it was not willing, then will would have occurred, and its occurrence in His essence is impossible, because He is not a locus for events, and its occurrence not in His essence does not make Him willing. Let us leave aside considering the locus of its occurrence: is not the problem standing in the very fact of its occurrence? And where did it occur? And why did it occur now and not occur before? Did it occur now from no direction of God? If an event is permissible without an originator, then let the world be an event without a maker, otherwise what difference between one event and another? And if it occurred by God's causing it to occur, then why did it occur now and not occur before? Was it due to absence of an instrument, or power, or purpose, or nature? So when that changed to existence it occurred? The same problem returns. Or due to absence of will? Then will would need another will, and likewise the first will, and it would regress infinitely. Thus it is verified absolutely that the emanation of the event from the eternal without a change in something of the eternal—in power, instrument, time, purpose, or nature—is impossible, and positing a change of state is impossible, because the discourse about that new change is the same as the discourse about other than it. A notice: how ugly is what is said: that high things strive to do something for what is below them because that is better for them, and so that they might be doers of the beautiful, or that the First Truth does something for the sake of something, and that His act has a 'why'. And all of that is impossible. And as long as the world exists and its coming to be is impossible, its eternity is established without doubt.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' argument that the world must be eternal because a new event (like creation) cannot originate from an eternal being without change. He begins by considering the problem of divine will: if God was not willing and then became willing, then a new will occurred in Him, which is impossible because He is not a subject for accidents. Even if the will occurs outside His essence, that does not make Him truly willing. Ghazali then says to set aside the locus and focus on the very occurrence of the event. He poses a series of rhetorical questions: where did it occur? Why now and not earlier? If it can happen without a cause, then the world needs no maker; if it happens by God's causing, why now? He lists possible reasons: absence of instrument, power, purpose, or nature. If these were absent before and then present, the same problem remains. If it is due to absence of will, then will itself would need another will, leading to an infinite regress. So the conclusion is that it is absolutely impossible for a new event to proceed from the eternal without a change in something like power, instrument, time, purpose, or nature. Positing a change in the eternal state is also impossible, as the same problem applies to that change. Then Ghazali adds a sharp notice ('tanbih'): it is ugly to say that high entities strive for what is below them for beauty's sake, or that the First Truth acts for a purpose, because that implies a 'why' for His act. All such explanations are impossible. Finally, he restates the core: if the world exists and its origination is impossible, then its eternity is necessarily proven. Note: This argument is the philosophers' own proof, which Ghazali here presents and deconstructs to show its flaws; he is not agreeing with their conclusion but rather showing that their reasoning leads to absurdities.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada: will)
القديم (al-qadim: the eternal)
الحادث (al-hadith: the event/incident)
المحال (al-muhal: the impossible)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul: infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali accepts the eternity of the world.
Correction: Ghazali is not accepting the eternity; he is showing that the philosophers' own premises lead to that conclusion, but those premises are flawed. The passage is part of a refutation, not an endorsement.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali in the tanbih, how does he describe the claim that the First Truth acts for a purpose?
A) Beautiful and praiseworthy.
B) Ugly and impossible.
C) Possible but not necessary.
D) A matter of faith.

Key Takeaway
Any change in the eternal is as impossible as the original event, and divine acts cannot be explained by purpose or beauty.

Two Requirements for Philosophers on Eternity of the World
Editor presents philosophers' conditional and two demands if they reject emanation depiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two requirements Ghazali places on the philosophers if they reject a certain depiction of the world, and why he considers their proof insufficient.

Core Idea
The philosophers' emanation story is not a rational proof for eternity.

In this passage, the editor (likely Ghazali) lays out the conditional position of the philosophers on the eternity of the world. He argues that if they reject a particular emanationist model, they must meet two demands: provide a valid proof for the eternity of the celestial spheres and reconcile their view with the Quran. He then dismisses their emanation account as mere storytelling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the editor structures the conditional and the two requirements.
The passage is an editorial summary, so it reflects the author's own critique.
2
Identify the two requirements
The passage states that if philosophers reject a certain depiction, they have two obligations: (1) provide a detailed rational proof for the eternity of the celestial world, (2) define their stance on conflicting Quranic verses. Find these in the text.
3
Examine the critique of the first requirement
The editor says that what philosophers offer is not a proof but a story-like emanation sequence. He claims they lack rational demonstration for each link. Observe how he dismisses their argument.
This highlights the perceived weakness of philosophical cosmology.
4
Understand the second requirement and reference
The editor mentions Ibn Sina's attempt in Risala Adhawiyya as an effort to address the Quranic verses. Note that this is a separate work, not elaborated here.
The passage ends by directing interested readers to that book.

فإذا ارتضى الفلاسفة تصوير العالم بالصورة التى أشرت إليها آنفاً ، فإنهم يخلصون من الصعوبة الثانية ، وتبقى فقط الصعوبة الأولى ، وعند ذلك يشركهم في موقفهم غيرهم من أمثال ابن تيمية الذاهب إلى قدم العالم. أما إذا رفضوا هذا التصوير فعليهم أمران : أولا : أن يقدموا دليلا منتجاً لقدم عالم الأفلاك بالتفصيل ثانياً : أن يحددوا موقفهم من الآيات القرآنية التي تتنافى مع هذا التصوير. أما بالنسبة للأمر الأول ، فكل مالهم بإزائه - بمقتضى اطلاعي طبعاً – إنما هو تصوير لكيفية صدور العقل الأول عن الله ، ثم صدور العقل الثاني والنفس الأولى والفلك الأول عن العقل الأول ، وهكذا إلى آخر السلسلة ، وهذا أشبه بالقصة منه بالاستدلال العقلى ، فلا يزال ينقصهم الاستدلال العقلى على قدم كل حلقة من هذه السلسلة . وأما بالنسبة للأمر الثانى ، فقد بذل ابن سينا بخصوصه محاولة بسطها في كتابه «رسالة أضحوية في أمر المعاد» الذي نشرته لأول مرة دار الفكر العربي بتحقيقنا، فليرجع إليه من شاء .
If the philosophers accept the depiction of the world in the form I alluded to earlier, then they escape the second difficulty, and only the first difficulty remains, and then they are joined in their position by others like Ibn Taymiyya who holds the eternity of the world. But if they reject this depiction, then they have two tasks: First, to provide a valid proof for the eternity of the celestial world in detail. Second, to define their stance regarding the Quranic verses that conflict with this depiction. As for the first task, all they have in response to it—to the best of my knowledge, of course—is only a depiction of how the first intellect emanates from God, then the emanation of the second intellect, the first soul, and the first heaven from the first intellect, and so on to the end of the chain. This is more like a story than a rational proof; they still lack a rational proof for the eternity of each link in this chain. As for the second task, Ibn Sina made an attempt regarding it, which he expounded in his book 'Risala Adhawiyya on the Matter of the Hereafter,' which I published for the first time by Dar al-Fikr al-Arabi with my editing; let whoever wishes refer to it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary of the philosophers' situation in the debate on the eternity of the world. It begins by presenting a conditional: if the philosophers accept a certain depiction of the world (likely the emanationist model), they avoid a second difficulty but still face a first, and are joined by Ibn Taymiyya who also holds eternity. If they reject this depiction, they face two requirements: first, to provide a detailed rational proof for the eternity of the celestial spheres; second, to reconcile their position with the Quranic verses that indicate temporal creation. The editor then comments on the first requirement, stating that the philosophers' account of emanation—from the first intellect through successive intellects and heavens—is more like a story than a rational demonstration, lacking proof for the eternity of each stage. Regarding the second requirement, the editor notes Ibn Sina's attempt in his Risala Adhawiyya and refers the reader to it. The passage highlights the perceived inadequacy of the philosophical proofs.

The Contradictory Conclusions on the World's Eternity
Editor extracts opposing conclusions from Ghazali and Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers and theologians arrive at opposed conclusions regarding the eternity of the world, and why possibility is excluded by both camps.

Core Idea
Two camps, one world: both impossible to the other.

This lesson distills the editor's summary of the core disagreement between philosophers and theologians. Both groups argue from their premises to mutually exclusive conclusions. The philosophers conclude that the world must be eternal; the theologians conclude it must have a beginning. Neither considers the possibility that the world might be contingent in a neutral sense. This passage sets the stage for understanding the stakes of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the editorial summary
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the editor presents the conclusions of both sides before explaining the theological argument against infinite regress.
2
Identify the two conclusions
Find where the editor states the philosophers' view and the theologians' view. Note that they are direct opposites.
3
Understand the theologians' argument
The passage includes a quotation from Ibn Sina about the theologians' reasoning: if the world were eternal, there would be an actual infinite series of past events, which is impossible. Examine this argument.
The concept of an actual infinite is central to the theologians' refutation.
4
Reflect on the exclusion of possibility
The editor ends by noting that possibility (al-imkan) has no share in either camp's consideration. Why do both sides reject a middle ground?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا والذي يخلص من دليل الفلاسفة - الذي رواه الغزالى هنا على لسانهم - لإبطال حدوث العالم : أن حدوث العالم - في نظر الفلاسفة - محال. كما أن الذى يخلص من قول الغزالي - فيما يأتى بعد قليل - من أن : قدم العالم يؤدى إلى إثبات دورات للفلك لا نهاية لإعدادها ، ولا حصر لآحادها ، أن لها سدساً، وربعاً، ونصفاً، . . . إلخ » و ما رواه ابن سينا - فى الإشارات الفصل الثاني عشر من النمط الخامس، طبع دار المعارف - عن المتكلمين من قولهم : إن الواجب لم يزل ولا وجود لشيء عنه ، ثم ابتدأ وأراد وجود شيء عنه . ولولا هذا لكانت أحوال متجددة من أصناف شتى فى الماضى لا نهاية لها موجودة بالفعل ؛ لأن كل واحد منها وجد ، فالكل وجد، فيكون لما لا نهاية له من أمور متعاقبة كلية منحصرة في الوجود . وكيف يمكن أن تكون حال من هذه الأحوال توصف بأنها لا تكون إلا بعد ما لا نهاية له ، فتكون موقوفة على ما لا نهاية له ، فينقطع إليها ما لا نهاية له. ثم كل وقت يتجدد يزداد عدد تلك الأحوال ، وكيف يزداد ما لا نهاية له . الذي يخلص من كل ذلك أن قدم العالم – في نظر المتكلمين – محال . هكذا يقف الطرفان المتناظران على طرفي نقيض ، فيرى أحدهما القول بوجوب قدم العالم ، واستحالة القول بالحدوث، ويرى الآخر القول بوجوب الحدوث ، واستحالة القدم . وليس للقول بالإمكان نصيب من اعتبارهما.
This is what is concluded from the philosophers' argument—which Ghazali narrated here on their behalf—to invalidate the temporality of the world: that the temporality of the world—in the philosophers' view—is impossible. And likewise, what is concluded from Ghazali's statement—coming shortly—that the eternity of the world leads to proving cycles of the heaven with no end to their numbers and no limit to their units, that it has a sixth, a fourth, a half, etc.” and what Ibn Sina narrated in al-Isharat, chapter twelve of the fifth pattern, Dar al-Ma'arif edition, from the theologians, their statement: 'The Necessary Being never ceased and there was no existence of anything from Him, then He began and willed the existence of something from Him.' Were it not for this, there would be perpetually renewed states of various kinds in the past existing actually with no end, because each one of them existed, so the whole existed; thus there would be for what has no end of successive universal things confined to existence. And how can a state among these states be described as not occurring except after what has no end, so it would be dependent on what has no end, and what has no end would terminate in it. Then every time that is renewed, the number of those states increases, and how can what has no end increase? What is concluded from all that is that the eternity of the world—in the theologians' view—is impossible. Thus the two opposing parties stand on opposite extremes: one sees the obligation of the eternity of the world and the impossibility of temporality, and the other sees the obligation of temporality and the impossibility of eternity. And the statement of possibility has no share in their consideration.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary of the necessary conclusions from the arguments of the philosophers (as narrated by Ghazali) and the theologians (as quoted from Ibn Sina). It begins by stating that the philosophers' argument for invalidating the temporality of the world leads to the conclusion that temporality is impossible in their view. Similarly, from Ghazali's argument (which the editor says will come later) and from Ibn Sina's citation of the theologians' argument, the conclusion is that the eternity of the world is impossible in the theologians' view. The editor then quotes from Ibn Sina's al-Isharat the theologians' argument: that the Necessary Being never ceased and there was no existence from Him, then He began and willed existence. Without that, there would be an infinite series of past states, leading to an actual infinite, which is impossible. Thus, the two camps stand in direct opposition: the philosophers see eternity as necessary and temporality as impossible, while the theologians see temporality as necessary and eternity as impossible. Possibility has no place in either view.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
حدوث العالم
المتكلمين
الفلاسفة
دورات الفلك
ما لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the debate between philosophers and theologians allows for a middle ground where the world could be possible (contingent) in a neutral sense, but this passage shows that both sides reject possibility entirely.
Correction: The editors explicitly state: 'And the statement of possibility has no share in their consideration.' Both camps hold that the world's existence is either necessary (philosophers) or originated (theologians), with no third option.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what are the two opposing conclusions drawn from the philosophers and the theologians?
A) Philosophers see temporality as necessary; theologians see eternity as necessary.
B) Philosophers see eternity as impossible; theologians see temporality as impossible.
C) Philosophers see eternity as necessary; theologians see temporality as necessary.
D) Both see possibility as the only valid position.

Key Takeaway
The debate on the world's eternity is a direct opposition: philosophers affirm eternity, theologians affirm temporality, and neither accepts possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you find it easier to connect with a God who created the world at a specific moment in time, or one who has always been with the world? How does your view of time affect your sense of His closeness?
?
The theologians argue that an eternal past leads to contradictions. Have you ever caught yourself assuming that time stretches back forever without a starting point? What would it mean for your personal accountability if the universe had no beginning?
?
Imagine you genuinely believed that the world could be eternal—how would that change your urgency about worship, repentance, or seeking God? Would you feel less need for a Creator who intervened at a starting point? Be specific.

First Objection: Artifacts and Instantaneous Creation
First objection and response in the eternity debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the world's eternity and the response that creation is instantaneous, unlike motion.

Core Idea
Creation is an instantaneous act, not a temporal process.

The debate opens with a classic objection: everything made is temporal. The response introduces a crucial distinction between creation and motion. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the key terms 'مصنوع' (artifact) and 'فعل آني' (instantaneous act).
The response hinges on the nature of divine action.
2
Distinguish creation from motion
Understand that motion requires time, but creation does not. This is the core of the response.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: if creation is instantaneous, does that make the world temporal or eternal? The argument suggests it could be eternal.
This sets the stage for further debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنصار القدم ردود مقنعة على حجج أنصار الحدوث: يقول هؤلاء: إن كل مصنوع فهو حادث، ويرد أولئك: إن هذا يصدق على المفعول بالحركة، الذي لا يوجد إلا عند نهاية الفعل، أما الخلق ففعل آني، وهو – إذن – لا يقتضى تقدم الفاعل على المفعول بالمدة. يقول أنصار الحدوث: إذا كان العالم مصنوعاً من العدم، فهو موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً. ويرد أنصار القدم: ليس القصد من المقدم أن العالم مصنوع بعد العدم، بل إنه ليس مصنوعاً من شيء. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان مساوياً لله في المدة. ويرد أنصار القدم: إن الوجود الإلهى حاصل كله دفعة واحدة، ووجود العالم حاصل بالتعاقب، فليست هناك مساواة. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان قد سبق هذا اليوم أيام لا متناهية، ولما كان بلغ إلى هذا اليوم، من حيث إن عبور اللامتناهي مستحيل. ويرد أنصار القدم: الانتقال يكون دائماً من طرف إلى آخر، وأى يوم ماض أخذت فالأيام التالية إلى يومنا هذا متناهية، وقد أمكن قطعها. وهذه الحجة إنما تنهض لو كان بين الطرفين أوساط لا متناهية. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم والتوليد قديمين لتقدم ناس في عدد لا متناه؛ ونفس الإنسان خالدة، فيلزم أن يوجد الآن بالفعل نفوس إنسانية في عدد لا متناه، ومحال أن عدد لا متناه بالفعل. ويرد أنصار القدم: إن هذه الحجة جزئية، ويمكننا أن نقول: إن العالم قديم، لا الإنسان. ويخرج القديس توما من هذه المناقشة بأن الحدوث لا يعلم إلا بالإيمان، وبأن في اعتبار ذلك فائدة لمن يدعى إثبات العقائد بالبرهان، فلا يأتى بحجج غير قاطعة لئلا يظن بنا أننا إنما نتمسك بالعقائد استناداً إلى مثل هذه الحجج. فما أظهر التمييز هذا بين الإيمان والعقل!
The proponents of eternity have convincing responses to the arguments of the proponents of temporality: The latter say: Every artifact is temporal, and the former respond: This is true of an effect through motion, which exists only at the end of the action; but creation is an instantaneous act, so it does not require the priority of the agent over the effect in time. The proponents of temporality say: If the world was made from nothing, then it exists after not existing. The proponents of eternity respond: The intention is not that the world was made after non-existence, but rather that it was not made from anything. The proponents of temporality say: If the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in duration. The proponents of eternity respond: The divine existence is all realized at once, and the world's existence is realized in succession, so there is no equality. The proponents of temporality say: If the world were eternal, then before this day there would have been infinite days, and it would not have reached this day, because traversing the infinite is impossible. The proponents of eternity respond: Transition is always from one end to another, and whichever past day you take, the days following it up to this day are finite, and they could be traversed. This argument only stands if there were infinite intermediate steps between the two ends. The proponents of temporality say: If the world and generation were eternal, then men would have preceded in infinite number; and the human soul is immortal, so it follows that there would now actually exist human souls in infinite number, and an actual infinite number is impossible. The proponents of eternity respond: This argument is particular, and we can say: The world is eternal, not man. And Saint Thomas emerges from this debate saying that temporality is known only by faith, and that reflecting on this usefulness to those who claim to prove doctrines by demonstration, so that they do not bring non-definitive arguments lest it be thought that we cling to doctrines based on such arguments. How great is this distinction between faith and reason!
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents four classic exchanges in the debate on the world's eternity. First objection: artifacts are temporal; answer: creation is instantaneous, not a motion. Second: if made from nothing, it must be temporal; answer: 'from nothing' does not mean temporal priority, but absence of pre-existing matter. Third: if eternal, it would equal God in duration; answer: God's existence is timeless, world's is successive. Fourth: infinite past days would make today unreachable; answer: any finite segment from a past day to today is finite. Also the argument from infinite souls: answer: only the world is eternal, not necessarily man. Aquinas concludes that only faith can affirm temporality, and using weak proofs harms religious belief.

Key Terms
مصنوع
فعل آني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All production requires time.
Correction: Divine creation is instantaneous, not subject to time.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does creation not require temporal priority?
A) Because the creator is timeless
B) Because creation is an instantaneous act
C) Because the world is made from nothing
D) Because motion is impossible for God

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because creation is not a temporal process but an instantaneous act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah creating the universe, do you unconsciously imagine a process unfolding in time? How does this lesson challenge you to correct that mental picture? What will you do the next time you reflect on creation to ensure you think of it as an instantaneous act?
?
This lesson distinguishes between motion-based causation and instantaneous creation. In your own life, have you ever assumed that because something requires time in human action, it must also require time for Allah? Why is that assumption dangerous, and how will you guard against it from now on?
?
The objection in this passage seems logical, yet it fails because of a subtle distinction. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs carefully, even when they seem obviously true, and let the precise words of revelation and reason correct you? What is one deeply held assumption about Allah's action that you are now willing to reconsider?

Second Objection: Creation from Nothing and Temporal Priority
Second objection and response on creation from nothing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that creation from nothing implies temporal priority and the response that 'from nothing' means no pre-existing matter, not temporal succession.

Core Idea
'From nothing' does not mean 'after nothing' in time.

The second objection argues that if the world was made from nothing, it must have come into existence after non-existence. The response clarifies a crucial distinction: 'from nothing' refers to the absence of preexisting matter, not a temporal sequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the phrasing: 'إذا كان العالم مصنوعاً من العدم، فهو موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً'. Understand the claim of temporal priority.
2
Analyze the response
The response says: 'ليس القصد من المقدم أن العالم مصنوع بعد العدم، بل إنه ليس مصنوعاً من شيء.' Focus on the distinction.
The key is the difference between 'after non-existence' and 'not from anything'.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect: does this response allow the world to be eternal? If 'from nothing' doesn't imply time, then the world could be eternally created from nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنصار القدم ردود مقنعة على حجج أنصار الحدوث: يقول هؤلاء: إن كل مصنوع فهو حادث، ويرد أولئك: إن هذا يصدق على المفعول بالحركة، الذي لا يوجد إلا عند نهاية الفعل، أما الخلق ففعل آني، وهو – إذن – لا يقتضى تقدم الفاعل على المفعول بالمدة. يقول أنصار الحدوث: إذا كان العالم مصنوعاً من العدم، فهو موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً. ويرد أنصار القدم: ليس القصد من المقدم أن العالم مصنوع بعد العدم، بل إنه ليس مصنوعاً من شيء. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان مساوياً لله في المدة. ويرد أنصار القدم: إن الوجود الإلهى حاصل كله دفعة واحدة، ووجود العالم حاصل بالتعاقب، فليست هناك مساواة. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان قد سبق هذا اليوم أيام لا متناهية، ولما كان بلغ إلى هذا اليوم، من حيث إن عبور اللامتناهي مستحيل. ويرد أنصار القدم: الانتقال يكون دائماً من طرف إلى آخر، وأى يوم ماض أخذت فالأيام التالية إلى يومنا هذا متناهية، وقد أمكن قطعها. وهذه الحجة إنما تنهض لو كان بين الطرفين أوساط لا متناهية. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم والتوليد قديمين لتقدم ناس في عدد لا متناه؛ ونفس الإنسان خالدة، فيلزم أن يوجد الآن بالفعل نفوس إنسانية في عدد لا متناه، ومحال أن عدد لا متناه بالفعل. ويرد أنصار القدم: إن هذه الحجة جزئية، ويمكننا أن نقول: إن العالم قديم، لا الإنسان. ويخرج القديس توما من هذه المناقشة بأن الحدوث لا يعلم إلا بالإيمان، وبأن في اعتبار ذلك فائدة لمن يدعى إثبات العقائد بالبرهان، فلا يأتى بحجج غير قاطعة لئلا يظن بنا أننا إنما نتمسك بالعقائد استناداً إلى مثل هذه الحجج. فما أظهر التمييز هذا بين الإيمان والعقل!
The proponents of eternity have convincing responses to the arguments of the proponents of temporality: The latter say: Every artifact is temporal, and the former respond: This is true of an effect through motion, which exists only at the end of the action; but creation is an instantaneous act, so it does not require the priority of the agent over the effect in time. The proponents of temporality say: If the world was made from nothing, then it exists after not existing. The proponents of eternity respond: The intention is not that the world was made after non-existence, but rather that it was not made from anything. The proponents of temporality say: If the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in duration. The proponents of eternity respond: The divine existence is all realized at once, and the world's existence is realized in succession, so there is no equality. The proponents of temporality say: If the world were eternal, then before this day there would have been infinite days, and it would not have reached this day, because traversing the infinite is impossible. The proponents of eternity respond: Transition is always from one end to another, and whichever past day you take, the days following it up to this day are finite, and they could be traversed. This argument only stands if there were infinite intermediate steps between the two ends. The proponents of temporality say: If the world and generation were eternal, then men would have preceded in infinite number; and the human soul is immortal, so it follows that there would now actually exist human souls in infinite number, and an actual infinite number is impossible. The proponents of eternity respond: This argument is particular, and we can say: The world is eternal, not man. And Saint Thomas emerges from this debate saying that temporality is known only by faith, and that reflecting on this usefulness to those who claim to prove doctrines by demonstration, so that they do not bring non-definitive arguments lest it be thought that we cling to doctrines based on such arguments. How great is this distinction between faith and reason!
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents four classic exchanges in the debate on the world's eternity. First objection: artifacts are temporal; answer: creation is instantaneous, not a motion. Second: if made from nothing, it must be temporal; answer: 'from nothing' does not mean temporal priority, but absence of pre-existing matter. Third: if eternal, it would equal God in duration; answer: God's existence is timeless, world's is successive. Fourth: infinite past days would make today unreachable; answer: any finite segment from a past day to today is finite. Also the argument from infinite souls: answer: only the world is eternal, not necessarily man. Aquinas concludes that only faith can affirm temporality, and using weak proofs harms religious belief.

Key Terms
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation from nothing necessarily means the world began to exist in time.
Correction: It only means no pre-existing material was used; temporal priority is not entailed.

Checkpoint
How do proponents of eternity reinterpret 'made from nothing'?
A) As meaning the world was made from a pre-existing substance
B) As meaning the world was made after nothing existed
C) As meaning the world was not made from anything
D) As meaning the world was made by a temporal process

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'made from nothing' does not imply a temporal beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on understanding Allah's creation through your own limited concepts of time and sequence? What would it mean for you to accept that Allah's power operates beyond such categories?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Samad' (He is Self-Sufficient), do you truly trust that He creates without needing any pre-existing 'raw material'? How does this lesson challenge you to rely on Him alone in your moments of need?
?
The objection tried to force a human logic onto divine action. Where in your life do you subtly limit Allah's absolute power by demanding that His work fit your understanding? Will you repent of that today and submit to His absolute creative authority?

Third Objection: Equality in Duration with God
Third objection and response on equality of duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that an eternal world would equal God in duration and the response that God's existence is timeless while the world's is successive.

Core Idea
God's existence is all at once; the world's is successive – no equality.

The third objection argues that if the world were eternal, it would share God's infinite duration, implying equality. The response distinguishes between God's timeless, non-successive existence and the world's temporal succession. This lesson explores that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic: 'لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان مساوياً لله في المدة' and the response contrasting 'الوجود الإلهى حاصل كله دفعة واحدة' with 'وجود العالم حاصل بالتعاقب'.
2
Understand the distinction
Grasp that God's existence is not in time; it is a single, unchanging present. The world's existence unfolds in time.
This avoids any notion of competition or equality.
3
Apply to the debate
Ask: does this response effectively refute the equality objection? Why or why not?
It shows that duration is not a predicate applicable to God in the same way.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live moment by moment, each successive instant. God sees your entire life as a single present act. Which moment of your past are you still trying to hide from Him, forgetting that He sees it as present? Will you repent now, not later?
?
If God's existence is not bound by time, then He is not waiting for you to 'get it together' tomorrow. What are you postponing that you would do if you truly believed His eternal now demands immediate response?
?
The world's succession means every moment passes and never returns. Are you using each passing moment to align with God's eternal will, or are you wasting time as if it were infinite?

Fourth Objection: Traversing Infinite Past Days
Fourth objection on infinite past days and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an infinite past makes today unreachable and the response that any segment from a past day to today is finite.

Core Idea
Traversing the infinite is impossible, but the past is not traversed all at once.

A classic argument against an eternal universe is that reaching today would require traversing an infinite number of past days. The response argues that we only traverse finite segments from any given past day to today. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims: if infinite past days existed, today could not be reached because traversing an infinite is impossible.
2
Analyze the response
The response: from any arbitrary past day to today, the days are finite. So the traversal is always finite.
The key is that we do not start from the beginning of infinity; there is no beginning.
3
Consider the counter
The response adds: the argument works only if there were infinite intermediate steps between two ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite past would make the present unreachable because you cannot traverse an infinite series.

According to the response, why is the objection about infinite days invalid?
A) Because the world is not eternal
B) Because days are not real
C) Because from any past day to today the days are finite
D) Because traversing the infinite is possible

An infinite past does not prevent reaching the present because the traversal is always between finite segments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that time is finite and each day is a gift, would you change how you spend your hours? What specific habit or relationship are you neglecting as if the days were infinite?
?
This lesson shows that from any moment you choose, the time to now is a finite, traversable segment. Are you aware that your past is closed and your remaining days are limited? How does that awareness change what you will do tomorrow?
?
The objection fails because there is no starting point in an infinite past—but your own life has a definite beginning. Does that fact weigh on you? If you knew your last day is near, what deed would you rush to complete today?

Fifth Objection: Infinite Souls and Aquinas's Conclusion
Fifth objection and Aquinas's appeal to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection from infinite souls and Aquinas's conclusion that the world's temporality is known only by faith.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot prove the world's temporality; faith is needed.

The final objection argues that an eternal world would lead to an actual infinite number of human souls, which is impossible. The response is that only the world is eternal, not necessarily man. Then Aquinas concludes that temporality is a matter of faith, not rational demonstration. This lesson ties the debate together with a methodological reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objection: if world and generation are eternal, infinite souls would exist now. Response: the argument is partial; we can say the world is eternal but not man.
2
Understand Aquinas's conclusion
Aquinas says that temporality is known only by faith, and that one should not use weak arguments because they undermine religious belief.
3
Reflect on the methodology
This challenges the idea that all religious truths can be rationally proved. Faith has its place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنصار القدم ردود مقنعة على حجج أنصار الحدوث: يقول هؤلاء: إن كل مصنوع فهو حادث، ويرد أولئك: إن هذا يصدق على المفعول بالحركة، الذي لا يوجد إلا عند نهاية الفعل، أما الخلق ففعل آني، وهو – إذن – لا يقتضى تقدم الفاعل على المفعول بالمدة. يقول أنصار الحدوث: إذا كان العالم مصنوعاً من العدم، فهو موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً. ويرد أنصار القدم: ليس القصد من المقدم أن العالم مصنوع بعد العدم، بل إنه ليس مصنوعاً من شيء. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان مساوياً لله في المدة. ويرد أنصار القدم: إن الوجود الإلهى حاصل كله دفعة واحدة، ووجود العالم حاصل بالتعاقب، فليست هناك مساواة. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم قديماً، لكان قد سبق هذا اليوم أيام لا متناهية، ولما كان بلغ إلى هذا اليوم، من حيث إن عبور اللامتناهي مستحيل. ويرد أنصار القدم: الانتقال يكون دائماً من طرف إلى آخر، وأى يوم ماض أخذت فالأيام التالية إلى يومنا هذا متناهية، وقد أمكن قطعها. وهذه الحجة إنما تنهض لو كان بين الطرفين أوساط لا متناهية. يقول أنصار الحدوث: لو كان العالم والتوليد قديمين لتقدم ناس في عدد لا متناه؛ ونفس الإنسان خالدة، فيلزم أن يوجد الآن بالفعل نفوس إنسانية في عدد لا متناه، ومحال أن عدد لا متناه بالفعل. ويرد أنصار القدم: إن هذه الحجة جزئية، ويمكننا أن نقول: إن العالم قديم، لا الإنسان. ويخرج القديس توما من هذه المناقشة بأن الحدوث لا يعلم إلا بالإيمان، وبأن في اعتبار ذلك فائدة لمن يدعى إثبات العقائد بالبرهان، فلا يأتى بحجج غير قاطعة لئلا يظن بنا أننا إنما نتمسك بالعقائد استناداً إلى مثل هذه الحجج. فما أظهر التمييز هذا بين الإيمان والعقل!
The proponents of eternity have convincing responses to the arguments of the proponents of temporality: The latter say: Every artifact is temporal, and the former respond: This is true of an effect through motion, which exists only at the end of the action; but creation is an instantaneous act, so it does not require the priority of the agent over the effect in time. The proponents of temporality say: If the world was made from nothing, then it exists after not existing. The proponents of eternity respond: The intention is not that the world was made after non-existence, but rather that it was not made from anything. The proponents of temporality say: If the world were eternal, it would be equal to God in duration. The proponents of eternity respond: The divine existence is all realized at once, and the world's existence is realized in succession, so there is no equality. The proponents of temporality say: If the world were eternal, then before this day there would have been infinite days, and it would not have reached this day, because traversing the infinite is impossible. The proponents of eternity respond: Transition is always from one end to another, and whichever past day you take, the days following it up to this day are finite, and they could be traversed. This argument only stands if there were infinite intermediate steps between the two ends. The proponents of temporality say: If the world and generation were eternal, then men would have preceded in infinite number; and the human soul is immortal, so it follows that there would now actually exist human souls in infinite number, and an actual infinite number is impossible. The proponents of eternity respond: This argument is particular, and we can say: The world is eternal, not man. And Saint Thomas emerges from this debate saying that temporality is known only by faith, and that reflecting on this usefulness to those who claim to prove doctrines by demonstration, so that they do not bring non-definitive arguments lest it be thought that we cling to doctrines based on such arguments. How great is this distinction between faith and reason!
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents four classic exchanges in the debate on the world's eternity. First objection: artifacts are temporal; answer: creation is instantaneous, not a motion. Second: if made from nothing, it must be temporal; answer: 'from nothing' does not mean temporal priority, but absence of pre-existing matter. Third: if eternal, it would equal God in duration; answer: God's existence is timeless, world's is successive. Fourth: infinite past days would make today unreachable; answer: any finite segment from a past day to today is finite. Also the argument from infinite souls: answer: only the world is eternal, not necessarily man. Aquinas concludes that only faith can affirm temporality, and using weak proofs harms religious belief.

Key Terms
عدد لا متناه بالفعل
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational arguments can decisively prove the world's temporality.
Correction: Aquinas argues that temporality is a matter of faith, not demonstration.

Checkpoint
According to Aquinas, how is the temporality of the world known?
A) Through rational demonstration
B) Through empirical observation
C) Only by faith
D) Through the infinite souls argument

Key Takeaway
The world's temporality cannot be proven by reason; it is an article of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present Islam to others, do you rely on arguments that you know are logically conclusive, or do you sometimes use weak reasoning that could discredit the faith? What would it take for you to humbly say 'I believe this by faith' even when reason cannot prove it?
?
Aquinas warned against bringing non-definitive arguments lest people think we cling to doctrines based on such arguments. Is there a belief you hold that you have tried to prove with shaky reasoning? Are you willing to drop that reasoning and simply trust the revelation, even if it feels less secure?
?
If the temporality of the world is known only by faith, then your entire worldview depends on a gift from God—not your intellect. Does that terrify you, or liberate you? How does this distinction between faith and reason change the way you engage with someone who demands a rational proof for every Islamic doctrine?

First Argument for Temporal Creation and Its Refutation
Dialectical exchange on temporal creation vs. pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between causation through movement and instantaneous creation, and why the argument from artifact does not prove temporal creation of the world.

Core Idea
Not all causation implies temporal priority; instantaneous creation bypasses time.

This lesson presents the first argument for the temporal creation of the world and its refutation by supporters of pre-eternity. The key move is a distinction between two types of causation: one requiring time (movement-based) and one instantaneous (divine creation). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the cosmological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the two opposing positions presented concisely.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage has two parts: the argument by supporters of ḥudūth (every artifact is temporal) and the response by supporters of qidam (distinction between maf‘ūl bi-l-ḥaraka and khalq). Identify each.
3
Understand the key distinction
The refutation hinges on differentiating between effects produced through movement (which require time) and instantaneous creation (which does not). Why is this distinction important?
Think about a craftsman vs. divine command 'Be!'
4
Apply the lesson
Explain in your own words why the argument 'every artifact is temporal' fails for divine creation according to this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنصار القدم ردود مقنعة على حجج يقول هؤلاء : إن كل مصنوع فهو حادث. ويرد أولئك : إن هذا يصدق على المفعول بالحركة ، الذي لا يوجد إلا عند نهاية الفعل أما الخلق ففعل آنى ، وهو - إذن - لا يقتضى تقدم الفاعل على المفعول بالمدة
And the supporters of pre-eternity have convincing responses to arguments. These [supporters of ḥudūth] say: Every artifact is temporal (ḥādith). And those [supporters of qidam] respond: This applies to that which is brought about by movement (maf‘ūl bi-l-ḥaraka), which exists only at the end of the action. As for creation (khalq), it is an instantaneous act, so it does not require the temporal priority of the agent over the effect.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the temporal creation of the world and its refutation. The supporters of temporal creation argue that everything that is made or manufactured is temporal. The supporters of pre-eternity respond by making a distinction between two types of causation: that which occurs through movement (maf‘ūl bi-l-ḥaraka) and that which is instantaneous creation (khalq). The former, like a craftsman producing a statue, requires time, so the effect exists after the action. But creation is an instantaneous act; it does not imply that the agent precedes the effect in time. Therefore, the argument that every artifact is temporal does not apply to divine creation, which is not a temporal process.

Key Terms
حدوث (ḥudūth)
قدم (qidam)
مصنوع (maṣnū‘)
حادث (ḥādith)
مفعول بالحركة (maf‘ūl bi-l-ḥaraka)
خلق (khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All causation requires temporal priority of cause over effect.
Correction: The passage shows that only causation through movement requires temporal priority; instantaneous creation (khalq) does not.

Checkpoint
According to the supporters of pre-eternity, why is the argument 'every artifact is temporal' not decisive?
A) Because artifacts can be eternal if made by God.
B) Because the argument only applies to effects produced through movement, not to instantaneous creation.
C) Because temporal priority is not required for any causation.
D) Because the world is not an artifact.

Key Takeaway
The argument for temporal creation from artifacts fails because divine creation is instantaneous, not a movement-based process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you think about God creating the universe, do you imagine a process with steps over time? How does the concept of instantaneous creation change how you relate to His power and your own need for patience?
?
This lesson reveals that our human reasoning can be limited when applied to God. In what area of your life are you demanding that God act according to your understanding of time and process? How will you surrender that today?

The Infinite Past Days Argument
If the world is eternal, an infinite past would be traversed; reply distinguishes finite vs. infinite intervals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against an eternal world based on the impossibility of traversing an infinite past, and the philosophical rebuttal that distinguishes between infinite and finite intervals.

Core Idea
The infinite past cannot be traversed if there is a starting point, but the present is not reached from a beginning.

Imam Ghazali presents one of the most famous arguments against the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal: if the world had no beginning, an infinite number of days would have to pass before reaching today, which is impossible. The philosophers respond by pointing out that the argument mistakenly assumes a starting point. This lesson unpacks both sides of this critical debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: argument then response.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'عبور اللامتناهي'.
2
Identify the two positions
Distinguish the argument from the supporters of temporal creation and the response from the supporters of pre-eternity.
3
Understand the concept of traversing the infinite
Reflect on why traversing an infinite series is considered impossible, and how the response reframes the issue.
Think about the difference between an infinite series with a start and one without.
4
Apply the distinction between infinite and finite intervals
Consider the response's claim that from any past day, the interval to today is finite. How does that refute the argument?
5
Summarize the logical force of each side
Write a brief summary of the debate in your own words.
Focus on the key insight: the argument only works if there are infinite middle terms between two fixed endpoints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول أنصار الحدوث : لو كان العالم قديماً ، لكان قد سبق هذا اليوم، أيام لامتناهية ، ولما كان بلغ إلى هذا اليوم ، من حيث إن عبور اللامتناهي مستحيل . ويرد أنصار القدم : الانتقال يكون دائماً من طرف إلى آخر ، وأى يوم ماض أخذت فالأيام التالية إلى يومنا هذا متناهية ، وقد أمكن قطعها . وهذه الحجة إنما تنهض لو كان بين الطرفين أوساط لا متناهية .
The supporters of ḥudūth say: If the world were eternal, then this day would have been preceded by infinite days, and it would not have reached this day, because traversing the infinite is impossible. And the supporters of qidam respond: Transition is always from one endpoint to another, and whichever past day you take, the days following it up to our day are finite, and they can be traversed. This argument only proceeds if there were infinite middle terms between the two endpoints.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage deals with the classic argument against an eternal world based on the impossibility of traversing an actual infinite. The supporters of temporal creation argue that if the world had an infinite past, we could never arrive at the present day, since an infinite series cannot be completed. The supporters of pre-eternity object that the argument commits a category error. When you pick any past day, the interval from that day to today is finite, not infinite. The infinite past does not have a starting point; there is no 'first' day. The argument mistakenly assumes a starting point and then an infinite gap. If there were infinite middle terms (i.e., an infinite number of days between two fixed points), it would be valid, but the infinite past is not like that.

Key Terms
لامتناهي (lā mutanāhī)
عبور اللامتناهي ('ubūr al-lā mutanāhī)
أوساط لا متناهية (awsāṭ lā mutanāhiya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal past would require an infinite traversal from the beginning of time to the present.
Correction: The response shows that there is no starting point; the infinite past has no beginning, so the traversal is not from a starting point but rather any interval from a past day to today is finite.

Checkpoint
How do the supporters of pre-eternity respond to the argument that an eternal world would require traversing an infinite number of days to reach the present?
A) They deny that the past is infinite.
B) They say that the days are finite if counted from any past day to today.
C) They argue that God can traverse the infinite.
D) They claim that the present day is the first day.

Key Takeaway
The objection to an eternal world fails because it presupposes a starting point for the infinite past; the response clarifies that any finite past interval is traversable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the possibility that the universe may have no beginning, does it shake the foundation of your belief in a Creator? Or does your faith rest on something deeper than this argument? Be honest: would you still believe even if this particular proof fails?
?
The response in this passage shows that an opponent's argument can be dismantled by exposing a hidden assumption. In your own life, are there assumptions about God or existence that you hold without examination? Are you willing to question them, or do you fear what you might find?
?
If the infinite past requires no starting point, what does that say about your own need for a 'starting point' in your spiritual journey? Are you waiting for a perfect moment or a clear sign, or can you move forward from where you are now, finite though it may be?

The Infinite Human Souls Argument
Ghazālī presents the infinite souls argument and the reply from supporters of pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite number of human souls argument challenges the claim of an eternal world and how supporters of pre-eternity can respond by separating human generation from the world's eternity.

Core Idea
An eternal world need not have eternal human generation.

This passage presents a key exchange in the debate over the world's pre-eternity. The proponents of temporal creation (ḥudūth) argue that if the world and human generation were both eternal, an actual infinite number of immortal souls would exist now – an impossibility. The defenders of pre-eternity (qidam) counter that the argument mistakenly assumes human generation is also eternal, when it may have begun later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument and reply as a unit.
2
Identify the key concepts
Note the terms tawlīd (generation), nafs khālida (immortal soul), and 'adad lā mutanāhī bil-fi‘l (actual infinite number).
These three terms carry the argument.
3
Understand the argument's structure
The proponents of ḥudūth present a conditional: if both world and generation are eternal → infinite souls now → impossible.
4
Examine the reply
The supporters of qidam reject the conditional by denying that human generation is eternal. They say the argument is particular (juz'iyya).
They can maintain that the world is eternal but human life began later.
5
Reflect on the implications
This shows that the infinite souls argument does not force the conclusion that the world is not eternal. The debate shifts to the beginning of human life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول أنصار الحدوث : لو كان العالم والتوليد قديمين لتقدم ناس في عدد لامتناه ؛ ونفس الإنسان خالدة ، فيلزم أن يوجد الآن بالفعل نفوس إنسانية في عدد لامتناه ، ومحال أن عدد لامتناه بالفعل . ويرد أنصار القدم : إن هذه الحجة جزئية ، ويمكننا أن نقول : إن العالم قديم ، لا الإنسان .
The supporters of ḥudūth say: If the world and generation (tawlīd) were both eternal, then people would have succeeded in an infinite number; and the human soul is immortal, so it would follow that actually existing human souls are infinite in number, and an actual infinite number is impossible. And the supporters of qidam respond: This argument is particular (juz’iyya), and we can say: The world is eternal, but not the human.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an argument based on the actual infinite. If the world and the process of human generation (tawlīd) are both eternal, then an infinite number of humans would have existed, each with an immortal soul. These souls would now coexist, constituting an actual infinite, which is impossible. The supporters of pre-eternity counter that this argument is particular (juz’iyya) because it assumes that human generation is also eternal. But they can maintain that the world is eternal while humans are not; i.e., human life and reproduction began at some point in time. Thus, the infinite souls argument does not force the conclusion that the world itself is not eternal.

Key Terms
توليد (tawlīd)
نفس خالدة (nafs khālida)
عدد لامتناه بالفعل ('adad lā mutanāhī bil-fi‘l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal world must imply eternal human generation.
Correction: The supporters of qidam can separate the two: the world is eternal, but human generation began at a later time.

Checkpoint
Why do the supporters of pre-eternity consider the infinite souls argument to be 'particular'?
A) Because it only applies to the human soul, not to all things.
B) Because it assumes that human generation is also eternal, which they can deny.
C) Because it relies on a particular interpretation of the soul.
D) Because it is a weak argument in itself.

Key Takeaway
The infinite souls argument does not prove the world's temporality because it can be avoided by denying that human generation is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the infinite souls argument, do you find yourself more persuaded by the logic of an actual infinite, or by the counter-argument that separates the world from human generation? Which side seems more consistent with your understanding of God's power?
?
If the supporters of pre-eternity are right that the world is eternal but humans began in time, what does that imply about your own dependence on God? Are you just a recent addition to an eternal cosmos, or does your existence still require a Creator?
?
The argument hinges on an assumption about human generation being eternal. What assumptions about your own life—your origins, purpose, or destiny—do you hold without examining? Would questioning them change how you relate to God?

Ghazali's First Objection: The Eternal Will Argument
Ghazali presents his first objection to the philosophers' strongest argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection to the philosophers' argument for the world's pre-eternity: that an eternal will can specify the moment of creation without any change in God.

Core Idea
An eternal will can choose a specific time without changing.

Ghazali begins his rebuttal by identifying the philosophers' most deceptive argument for the world's pre-eternity. He then presents his first objection: why can't one believe that the world was created at a specific time by an eternal will that simply willed it at that moment? This objection challenges the assumption that an eternal cause must produce an eternal effect, preserving divine immutability while allowing temporal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic text from 'فهذا أخيل أدلتهم' to 'فما المانع من هذا الاعتقاد وما المحيل له ؟ !'. Notice how Ghazali sets up the philosophers' strongest argument and then immediately presents his objection.
The phrase 'فهذا أخيل أدلتهم' indicates the argument is deceptive, not necessarily false.
2
Identify the believer's creed
Find the formulation of the believer's position: the world came about by an eternal will that necessitated its existence at a specific time, with no change in God. This is Ghazali's own theological framework.
3
Understand the challenge
Ghazali asks the philosophers: 'What prevents this belief and what makes it impossible?' This forces them to justify their presumptions about causality.
This is a direct challenge to the philosophical axiom that a complete cause must produce its effect immediately.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is powerful: it shows that the philosophers' argument against creation in time assumes that God's will operates like a natural cause, but will is free and can specify without change.
This connects to the broader Islamic doctrine of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا أخيل أدلتهم ، وبالجملة كلامهم فى سائر مسائل الإلهيات أرك من كلامهم في هذه المسألة ، إذ يقدرون ههنا على فنون من التخييل لا يتمكنون منها في غيرها ، فلذلك قدمنا هذه المسألة ، وقدمنا أقوى أدلتهم الاعتراض من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يقال : بم تنكرون على من يقول : إن العالم حدث بإرادة قديمة ، اقتضت وجوده فى الوقت الذى وجد فيه ، وأن يستمر العدم إلى الغاية التي استمر إليها ، وأن يبتدئ الوجود من حيث ابتدأ ، وأن الوجود قبله لم يكن مراداً فلم يحدث لذلك ، وأنه فى وقته الذى حدث فيه مراد بالإرادة القديمة ، فحدث لذلك ، فما المانع من هذا الاعتقاد وما المحيل له ؟ ! فإن قيل : هذا محال بين الإحالة ، لأن الحادث موجب ومسبب، وكما يستحيل حادث بغير سبب وموجب ، يستحيل وجود موجب قد تم بشرائط إيجابه وأركانه وأسبابه ، حتى لم يبق شيء منتظر البتة ، ثم يتأخر الموجب ؛ بل وجود الموجب عند تحقق الموجب بتمام شروطه ، ضرورى ، وتأخره محال ، وجود الحادث الموجب بلا موجب فقبل وجود العالم كان المريد موجوداً ، والإرادة موجودة ، ونسبتها إلى المراد حسب استحالة موجودة ، ولم يتجدد مريد ، ولم تتجدد إرادة ، ولا تجدد للإرادة نسبة لم تكن فإن كل ذلك تغير ، فكيف تجدد المراد ، وما المانع من التجدد قبل ذلك ؟ ! وحال التجدد لم يتميز عن الحال السابق في شيء من الأشياء ، وأمر من الأمور ، وحال من الأحوال، ونسبة من النسب ، بل الأمور كما كانت بعينها ثم لم يكن يوجد المراد ، وبقيت بعينها كما كانت ، فوجد المراد ، ما هذا إلا غاية الإحالة . وليس استحالة هذا الجنس ، فى الموجب والموجب الضروري الذاتي ، بل فى العرفى والوضعى ، فإن الرجل لو تلفظ بطلاق زوجته ، ولم تحصل البينونة في الحال ، لم يتصور أن تحصل بعده ، لأنه جعل اللفظ علة للحكم بالوضع
This is the most deceptive of their arguments. In general, their speech in other topics of theology is weaker than their speech on this issue, because here they can employ arts of deception that they cannot in others. Therefore we have given precedence to this issue and presented their strongest arguments. The objection is from two aspects: One of them: It is said: What do you have against someone who says: The world came about by an eternal will (irāda qadīma) that necessitated its existence at the time it existed, and that non-existence continued until the limit it continued to, and that existence began from where it began, and that existence before that was not willed, so it did not come about, and that at the time it came about it was willed by the eternal will, so it came about for that reason. So what prevents this belief and what makes it impossible? If it is said: This is an obvious impossibility, because the temporal event (ḥādith) is an effect and caused (mūjab wa musabbab). Just as it is impossible for a temporal event without a cause and necessary condition, it is impossible for a cause whose conditions of causation are complete, its elements and causes, so that nothing remains awaiting at all, and then the effect is delayed. Rather, the existence of the effect when the cause is realized with all its conditions is necessary, and its delay is impossible. The existence of the temporal effect without a cause [is impossible]. So before the world existed, the willer (murīd) existed, the will existed, and its relation to the willed was existent according to impossibility? No new willer came to be, no new will came to be, and no new relation of the will came to be that did not exist, for all that would be change. So how did the willed come to be? And what prevented it from coming to be before that? The state of coming to be is not distinguished from the previous state in anything, any matter, any condition, or any relation. Rather, the matters were exactly the same, then the willed did not exist, and they remained exactly the same, then the willed existed. This is nothing but the ultimate impossibility. And the impossibility of this kind is not in the necessary essential cause and effect, but rather in conventional and posited [causation]. For if a man pronounces divorce of his wife, and the separation does not occur immediately, it is inconceivable that it occurs afterwards, because he made the utterance a cause for the ruling by convention.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Ghazali's direct engagement with the philosophers' strongest argument for the pre-eternity of the world. He states that this is their most deceptive argument and that they are more skilled on this topic than on others. He then presents the first of his two objections. The objection is a theological one: why can't one believe that the world was created at a specific time by an eternal will, without any change in God? The philosophers' argument that the world must be eternal because an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect is false because the will can specify a particular time. Ghazali then anticipates a counter-objection from the philosophers: they would say that this is impossible because the causal nexus requires necessity—once all conditions are present, the effect must exist immediately. They argue that if the willer, will, and relation were all eternal, the world should have existed from eternity. The non-existence of the world before its creation indicates a change in the cause, which is impossible in God. Ghazali's response is that the impossibility here is not of the kind of necessary causal determination but of conventional (ʿurfī) and posited (waḍ‘ī) causation, like the conventional link between the utterance of divorce and the legal effect. He gives the example of a man who pronounces divorce but the separation does not happen immediately; that would be impossible only because the convention stipulates immediate effect. But the divine will is not bound by such conventions. The implication is that the philosophers have confused metaphysical necessity with conventional causation. Ghazali's full argument is subtle: he does not deny that God's will is eternal and immutable, but he asserts that the will can be directed to a particular moment without change in the will itself, because the volitional act of willing a specific time is not a change in the essence of the will. The passage ends with the conventional divorce example to illustrate the distinction.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة
تخييل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an eternal will must produce an eternal effect, because a cause that is always present should always have its effect.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the will is not a natural cause; it can freely specify a particular time without any change in itself. The absence of effect before that time is due to the will not willing it, not a deficiency in the cause.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali ask the philosophers in his first objection?
A) Why did God create the world at that particular time?
B) What prevents the belief that the world was created at a specific time by an eternal will?
C) How can an eternal will be free?
D) Why is the philosophers' argument deceptive?

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first objection preserves divine immutability while allowing creation in time by asserting that an eternal will can freely choose the moment of creation.

How does that truth challenge your tendency to ration your trust in His decree only when it makes sense to you? What one area of your life do you need to submit to the reality that His will chose a specific moment for your trial or blessing, and you need to accept it without second-guessing?
?
When you consider that God's eternal will has already determined every event in your life—including this very moment of reading this lesson—do you feel a sense of peace or a sense of unease? If it is unease, what does that reveal about your heart's attachment to controlling your own timeline? Will you today surrender the illusion that you could have changed God's timing for your circumstances?

The philosophers argued that an eternal will must produce an eternal effect; but you now know that God's will can specify a time without changing. How does this free you from the fear that God is trapped by necessity? In what specific situation do you need to stop acting as if God's hand is tied by your logic or your expectations, and instead wait patiently for the moment He has eternally willed for you?

Philosophers' Counter-Objection and Ghazali's Response: Necessary vs. Conventional Causation
Ghazali presents the philosophers' counter-objection and his response distinguishing necessary from conventional causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' main counter-objection to Ghazali's argument and his distinction between necessary essential causation and conventional/posited causation, using the divorce example.

Core Idea
Divine causation is unlike human conventional causation.

After presenting his first objection, Ghazali anticipates the philosophers' response: that a complete cause must produce its effect immediately. He then rebuts by distinguishing between necessary essential causation (like fire burning) and conventional or posited causation (like divorce by utterance). The example of a man pronouncing divorce that does not take effect immediately illustrates that the philosophers' objection only applies to conventional rules, not to God's creative will.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection and response
Read the Arabic text from 'فإن قيل : هذا محال بين الإحالة' to the end. First, understand the philosophers' objection: they claim that a complete cause with all conditions fulfilled must produce its effect immediately; otherwise it's impossible.
The philosophers' argument is based on the principle of sufficient reason: if all conditions are present, the effect must be present.
2
Identify Ghazali's distinction
Find where Ghazali says 'والاستحالة من هذا الجنس ليست في الموجب الضروري الذاتي بل في العرفي والوضعي'. He is distinguishing two types of causation: essential and conventional.
This is the core of his response.
3
Understand the divorce example
Ghazali gives the example of a man who says 'you are divorced' but the separation does not occur immediately. That would be impossible only because of the conventional legal rule. The divine will is not bound by such conventions.
This example shows that the philosophers' argument only works for man-made conventions, not for God's absolute power.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this response undermines the philosophers' necessitarianism. It shows that their objection assumes a mistaken analogy between divine and human causation.
This is a key move in the Tahafut.

فهذا أخيل أدلتهم ، وبالجملة كلامهم فى سائر مسائل الإلهيات أرك من كلامهم في هذه المسألة ، إذ يقدرون ههنا على فنون من التخييل لا يتمكنون منها في غيرها ، فلذلك قدمنا هذه المسألة ، وقدمنا أقوى أدلتهم الاعتراض من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يقال : بم تنكرون على من يقول : إن العالم حدث بإرادة قديمة ، اقتضت وجوده فى الوقت الذى وجد فيه ، وأن يستمر العدم إلى الغاية التي استمر إليها ، وأن يبتدئ الوجود من حيث ابتدأ ، وأن الوجود قبله لم يكن مراداً فلم يحدث لذلك ، وأنه فى وقته الذى حدث فيه مراد بالإرادة القديمة ، فحدث لذلك ، فما المانع من هذا الاعتقاد وما المحيل له ؟ ! فإن قيل : هذا محال بين الإحالة ، لأن الحادث موجب ومسبب، وكما يستحيل حادث بغير سبب وموجب ، يستحيل وجود موجب قد تم بشرائط إيجابه وأركانه وأسبابه ، حتى لم يبق شيء منتظر البتة ، ثم يتأخر الموجب ؛ بل وجود الموجب عند تحقق الموجب بتمام شروطه ، ضرورى ، وتأخره محال ، وجود الحادث الموجب بلا موجب فقبل وجود العالم كان المريد موجوداً ، والإرادة موجودة ، ونسبتها إلى المراد حسب استحالة موجودة ، ولم يتجدد مريد ، ولم تتجدد إرادة ، ولا تجدد للإرادة نسبة لم تكن فإن كل ذلك تغير ، فكيف تجدد المراد ، وما المانع من التجدد قبل ذلك ؟ ! وحال التجدد لم يتميز عن الحال السابق في شيء من الأشياء ، وأمر من الأمور ، وحال من الأحوال، ونسبة من النسب ، بل الأمور كما كانت بعينها ثم لم يكن يوجد المراد ، وبقيت بعينها كما كانت ، فوجد المراد ، ما هذا إلا غاية الإحالة . وليس استحالة هذا الجنس ، فى الموجب والموجب الضروري الذاتي ، بل فى العرفى والوضعى ، فإن الرجل لو تلفظ بطلاق زوجته ، ولم تحصل البينونة في الحال ، لم يتصور أن تحصل بعده ، لأنه جعل اللفظ علة للحكم بالوضع

In general, their speech in other topics of theology is weaker than their speech on this issue, because here they can employ arts of deception that they cannot in others.
